6 min
The Third Opinion and Its Refutation
Sanusi states the third opinion and immediately refutes it with a logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion in the discussion of necessary beliefs and its refutation based on the contradiction between necessity and obligation.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge cannot be obligatory, so the third opinion is self-contradictory.

In this lesson, we examine the third opinion held by al-Jahiz that the foundational beliefs required for taklif are known necessarily. Sanusi then presents a refutation based on a logical contradiction: if these beliefs were necessary, they would be beyond human control and thus cannot be obligatory—yet knowledge of God is unanimously agreed to be obligatory. Understanding this refutation clarifies why necessity and obligation are mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion statement
Read the opening lines describing the third opinion: that what is necessary for the mukallaf regarding matters like proving the Creator and prophethood is known necessarily.
Attribute to al-Jahiz and his followers.
2
Understand the refutation
Notice the refutation begins with the premise that knowledge of God is wajib (by consensus or reason). Then it argues that if it were necessary (daruri), it would be beyond one's power (ghayr maqdur), and nothing beyond power can be wajib.
This creates a contradiction (khalaf).
3
Connect to the broader debate
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter? It shows that necessary knowledge is involuntary, while obligated knowledge must be voluntary and attainable through effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثالث في هذه المسألة ( أن ما اعتقاده (لازم) للمكلف مما يتوقف عليه إثبات التكليف والعلم به ) نحو إثبات الصانع وصفاته والنبوات ضروري ) قيل: هذا مذهب الجاحظ ومن تابعه ( ويبطله أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة إجماعاً ) كما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة ) أو عقلا كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة فلو كانت ضرورية لكانت غير مقدور عليها ( ولا شيء من غير المقدور كذلك أي بواجب فلو كانت المعرفة ضرورية لم تكن واجبة هذا خلف ( احتج) لهذا المذهب ( بأنه ( أي بأن ذلك اللازم المذكور ( لو لم يكن حاصلاً) بالضرورة، بل كان نظرياً يتوقف حصوله على النظر ( كان العبد مكلفا بتحصيله ( بنظره ليثبت به الشرائع والأحكام التكليفية ( وأنه ) أي التكليف بتحصيله ( تكليف الغافل لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور المذكورة من نحو إثبات الصانع وصفاته والنبوات لا يعلم التكليف (قطعاً لا بهذه الأمور ولا بغيرها، وإذا لم يعلم التكليف أصلاً كان غافلاً، وتكليف الغافل لا يجوز إجماعاً (والجواب أن الغافل) الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً ( من لا يفهم الخطاب ( أصلاً كالصبي والمجنون (أو) يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف كالذي لم تبلغه دعوة نبي قطعا فإن هذين غافلان عن تصور التكليف بالتنبيه عليه فلا تكليف على الأول اتفاقاً، ولا على عندنا ( لا) من لا يعلم أنه مكلف مع أنه خوطب بكونه مكلفاً حال ما كان الثاني فاهما، فإنه غافل عن التصديق بالتكليف لا عن تصوره، وذلك لا يمنع من تكليفه ( وإلا لم يكن الكفار مكلفين إذ ليسوا مصدقين بالتكليف ( ولأن )
The third opinion in this issue (that what belief is necessary for the legally responsible person, of that on which depends the establishment of legal obligation and knowledge of it, such as proving the Creator and His attributes and prophecies, is necessary). It is said: this is the doctrine of al-Jahiz and his followers. And it is refuted by the fact that knowledge of God Almighty is obligatory by consensus, as the Ash'aris hold, or by reason, as the Mu'tazila hold. For if it were necessary, it would be beyond one's power, and nothing beyond one's power is obligatory. So if knowledge were necessary, it would not be obligatory; this is contradictory. An argument for this doctrine is that if the aforementioned necessity were not obtained necessarily, but were theoretical, its acquisition depending on reflection, then the servant would be obligated to acquire it through his reflection to thereby establish the laws and obligatory rulings. And that, i.e., the obligation to acquire it, is an obligation of the heedless, because he who does not know these mentioned matters, such as proving the Creator and His attributes and prophecies, does not know legal obligation at all, neither through these matters nor through others. And if he does not know legal obligation at all, he is heedless, and the obligation of the heedless is not permissible by consensus. The answer is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus is he who does not understand the address at all, like a child or an insane person, or he who understands but has not been told that he is obligated, like one whom a prophet's call has not reached at all. For these two are heedless of the conception of legal obligation due to absence of attention. So there is no obligation on the first by agreement, nor on the second according to us. Not he who does not know that he is obligated while having been addressed as being obligated while he was understanding; for he is heedless of the assent to legal obligation, not of its conception. And that does not prevent his obligation. Otherwise, the disbelievers would not be obligated, since they do not assent to legal obligation. And because...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion in the discussion of the necessary beliefs, its refutation, the argument for it, and the response. The third opinion holds that what the legally responsible person (mukallaf) must believe regarding matters on which the establishment of taklif (legal obligation) and knowledge of it depend, such as proving the existence of the Creator, His attributes, and prophethood, is necessary (daruri) knowledge. This is attributed to al-Jahiz and his followers. The refutation (ibṭāl) is given: knowledge of God is wajib (obligatory) by consensus (for Ash'aris) or by reason (for Mu'tazila). If it were necessary, it would be beyond one's power (ghayr maqdur), and nothing beyond one's power is wajib. Therefore, if knowledge were necessary, it would not be wajib—a contradiction (khalaf). Then an argument (hujja) is presented for the third opinion: if those necessary matters were not obtained necessarily but were theoretical (naẓarī), requiring reflection (naẓar) to obtain, then the servant would be obligated to acquire them through reflection to establish the laws and obligations, and that would be a taklif of the heedless (ghāfil). This is because anyone who does not know these matters does not know taklif at all. The response (jawāb) defines the heedless whose obligation is not permissible: either one who does not understand the address (khiṭāb) at all (like a child or insane), or one who understands but has not been told he is obligated (like one not reached by a prophet's call). These two are heedless of the conception (taṣawwur) of taklif. However, one who does not know he is obligated despite being addressed while understanding is heedless of the assent (taṣdīq) to taklif, not of its conception, and that does not prevent his obligation; otherwise, disbelievers would not be obligated since they do not assent to taklif.

Key Terms
ضروري
واجب
مكلف
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that necessary knowledge can also be obligatory because it is essential for faith.
Correction: Necessary knowledge is involuntary and beyond one's power, while obligation requires voluntary acquisition, making them incompatible.

Checkpoint
What is the core logical contradiction in the refutation of the third opinion?
A) Necessary knowledge is impossible, so it cannot exist.
B) Knowledge of God is wajib, but if it were necessary it would be beyond one's power and thus not wajib.
C) Al-Jahiz denied the existence of God.
D) The Ash'aris and Mu'tazila disagree on the basis of obligation.

Key Takeaway
The third opinion collapses because necessary knowledge cannot be wajib, yet knowledge of God is wajib by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that knowing Allah is something you already have 'by nature,' without effort? Yet this lesson says it is wajib upon you—so are you actively seeking it with the same urgency you would feel for a legal duty?
?
If necessary knowledge cannot be obligatory because it is beyond your control, then what about the knowledge you can control? Are you neglecting the study of aqeedah because you think it is 'automatic' or 'too deep'? What one concrete step will you take today to pursue the obligatory knowledge of Allah?

The Argument for Necessity and the Response
Sanusi presents an argument for the third opinion and then responds with a refined definition of the heedless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if foundational beliefs were not necessary, then obligation would be taklif of the heedless, and the nuanced response distinguishing heedlessness of conception vs. assent.

Core Idea
The heedless who cannot be obligated are those unaware of the very concept of obligation, not those who reject it.

Having refuted the third opinion on logical grounds, Sanusi now presents an argument that could be used to defend it: if those foundational beliefs were theoretical (naẓarī) rather than necessary, then the obligation to acquire them would amount to burdening the heedless (ghāfil). The response carefully defines 'heedless' in a way that avoids this charge, showing that the disbelievers are still obligated because they are only heedless of assent, not conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for the third opinion
Read the argument starting with 'احتج' : if the necessary beliefs were not obtained necessarily but required reflection, then the servant would be obligated to reflect, but this would be taklif of the heedless because the person does not know taklif at all.
This argument tries to show that obligation without prior necessary knowledge is absurd.
2
Understand the response's definition
Read the response carefully. It defines the ghāfil as either one who does not understand the address (khiṭāb) at all (child/insane), or one who understands but has not been told he is obligated (unreached by prophet). These two are heedless of conception (taṣawwur) of taklif.
The key distinction: conception vs. assent.
3
Apply the distinction
The one who does not know he is obligated despite being addressed while understanding—that person is heedless of assent (taṣdīq), not conception. This does not prevent obligation; otherwise disbelievers would not be obligated.
This resolves the argument and defends rational obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثالث في هذه المسألة ( أن ما اعتقاده (لازم) للمكلف مما يتوقف عليه إثبات التكليف والعلم به ) نحو إثبات الصانع وصفاته والنبوات ضروري ) قيل: هذا مذهب الجاحظ ومن تابعه ( ويبطله أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة إجماعاً ) كما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة ) أو عقلا كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة فلو كانت ضرورية لكانت غير مقدور عليها ( ولا شيء من غير المقدور كذلك أي بواجب فلو كانت المعرفة ضرورية لم تكن واجبة هذا خلف ( احتج) لهذا المذهب ( بأنه ( أي بأن ذلك اللازم المذكور ( لو لم يكن حاصلاً) بالضرورة، بل كان نظرياً يتوقف حصوله على النظر ( كان العبد مكلفا بتحصيله ( بنظره ليثبت به الشرائع والأحكام التكليفية ( وأنه ) أي التكليف بتحصيله ( تكليف الغافل لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور المذكورة من نحو إثبات الصانع وصفاته والنبوات لا يعلم التكليف (قطعاً لا بهذه الأمور ولا بغيرها، وإذا لم يعلم التكليف أصلاً كان غافلاً، وتكليف الغافل لا يجوز إجماعاً (والجواب أن الغافل) الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً ( من لا يفهم الخطاب ( أصلاً كالصبي والمجنون (أو) يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف كالذي لم تبلغه دعوة نبي قطعا فإن هذين غافلان عن تصور التكليف بالتنبيه عليه فلا تكليف على الأول اتفاقاً، ولا على عندنا ( لا) من لا يعلم أنه مكلف مع أنه خوطب بكونه مكلفاً حال ما كان الثاني فاهما، فإنه غافل عن التصديق بالتكليف لا عن تصوره، وذلك لا يمنع من تكليفه ( وإلا لم يكن الكفار مكلفين إذ ليسوا مصدقين بالتكليف ( ولأن )

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that a person who does not know taklif at all cannot be held accountable, but the response shows only those ignorant of the concept itself are exempt.
Correction: Ignorance of assent does not remove obligation; only ignorance of the conception of taklif does.

Checkpoint
According to the response, which type of heedlessness does not prevent obligation?
A) Heedlessness of conception (taṣawwur)
B) Heedlessness of assent (taṣdīq)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The argument for necessary knowledge is neutralized by distinguishing between heedlessness of concept vs. heedlessness of assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard the call of Islam countless times. In which areas of your life are you 'heedless of assent'—knowing you are obligated but refusing to submit? What would it take for you to move from conception to true assent today?
?
This lesson shows that being informed of obligation makes you accountable, even if you reject it. How often do you treat a known sin as if it were a minor oversight? Will you own your accountability and repent now?
?
When you stand before Allah, the excuse 'I did not know' will not apply to you because you have understood the address. Are you living as someone who truly believes he is obligated, or as someone who merely knows the concept? What specific change will you make to align your life with that obligation?

The Jahmiyya Claim and Refutation by Inner Sense
States the Jahmiyya claim and begins refutation with inner sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jahmiyya position that all knowledge is theoretical (nazari) and its refutation through the testimony of inner sense (wijdan) which proves the existence of necessary knowledge (daruri).

Core Idea
Inner sense directly refutes the claim that all knowledge is theoretical.

In this passage, the author identifies a fourth weak doctrine from the Jahmiyya who assert that all knowledge, without exception, is theoretical. He begins his refutation by appealing to the undeniable testimony of inner sense, which shows that some knowledge is inherently necessary and cannot be acquired through reflection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Jahmiyya claim
Read the opening sentence stating the fourth madhhab: that all knowledge, whether concept or assent, is nazari.
The claim is absolute — it includes even knowledge that is necessarily believed.
2
Understand the refutation by inner sense
The author says the claim is refuted by 'shahadat al-wijdan' — the testimony of inner sense. Notice that this is presented as a direct counterexample.
Inner sense is the immediate awareness we have of some things being self-evident.
3
Grasp the meaning of necessary knowledge
The commentary defines 'daruri' as that which the soul cannot separate itself from, emphasizing the impossibility of separation that is within divine power.
This clarifies the nature of necessary knowledge and why it contradicts the Jahmiyya claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(المذهب الرابع) في هذه المسألة ( إن الكل نظري) سواء كان تصوراً أو تصديقاً مما يلزم اعتقاده أو لا يلزم ) وهو مذهب بعض الجهمية ) التابعين لجهم بن صفوان الترمذي رئيس الجبرية ويبطله) ما مر من شهادة الوجدان بكون البعض ضرورياً، و من لزوم الدور أو التسلسل على تقدير كون الكل نظرياً ) قوله : ( ويبطله ) أي كون الكل نظرياً بالمعنى الذي مر في تحرير محل النزاع، حيث قال : نقض مذاهب ضعيفة في هذه المسألة فإن فسر النظري بمعنى ما يتوقف على شيء كان خروجاً عن محل النزاع. قوله : ( بأن الضروري الخ لأن الضروري ما يلزم نفس المخلوق لزوماً لا يجد إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً ، واللزوم هو امتناع الانفكاك ولم يفهموا أن المراد منه امتناع الانفكاك المقدور. قوله : فلو) توقف وقوعه على العلم به لزم الدور) قد يدفع الدور بأن مدعى الجاحظ و متبعيه هو أن الموقوف عليه لوقوع التكليف هو التصديق بإمكان التكليف، وهو لا يتوقف على الوقوع بل الإمكان الذي ليس الوقوع شرطاً لثبوته فلا دور وأنت خبير بأن تطبيق عبارة الكتاب على هذا، وإن أمكن بحمله على حذف المضاف في قوله لا يعلم التكليف، أي لا يعلم إمكان التكليف إلا أنه لا يتم حينئذ قوله : لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور لا يعلم إمكان التكليف، لأن العلم بإمكان التكليف لا يتوقف على تحقق التصديق بالأمور المذكورة بالفعل، وإنما يتوقف عليه العلم بوقوع التكليف بالفعل فتدبر. قوله : ( ويبطله) ما مر من شهادة الوجدان فإن قلت : شهادة الوجدان وحديث الدور والتسلسل إنما كان في النظر الذي يحتاج إلى النظر لا فيما يتناوله وغيره من التجربيات والوجدانيات وغيرهما فالإبطال بالشهادة ولزوم الدور والتسلسل على تقدير كون الكل نظرياً، بالمعنى الذي يظهر من الاحتجاج ممنوع قلت : لعل الجهمية زعموا أن الكل نظري بالمعنى المذكور فيما سبق، المقابل للضروري توهماً أن الضروري ما لا تجد النفس إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً، وما من علم إلا والنفس خالية عنه في مبدأ الفطرة فرد عليهم أولاً بأن مدعاهم أعني نظرية الكل بالمعنى المشهور يكذبه الوجدان، وثانياً بأن دليلهم لا يفيد ذلك وقد يقال : النزاع لفظي وأن مرادهم بالضروري الذي نفوه بالكلية ما لا يتوقف على أمر أصلاً، وبالنظري الذي أثبتوه ما يتوقف على شيء في الجملة فتأمل . منهم
(The fourth madhhab) in this issue (is that all is theoretical [nazari]), whether it is a concept or an assent, whether it is necessary to believe or not. And it is the madhhab of some of the Jahmiyya, the followers of Jahm ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi, the head of the Jabriyya. And it is refuted by what has passed of the testimony of inner sense (shahadat al-wijdan) that some things are necessary (daruri), and by the necessity of circularity (dawr) or regress (tasalsul) on the supposition that all is theoretical. His saying: 'And it is refuted' means that all is theoretical in the sense that was mentioned in the clarification of the point of dispute, where he said: refuting weak doctrines in this issue, for if theoretical is interpreted as that which depends on something, it would be a departure from the point of dispute. His saying: 'Because the necessary (al-daruri) ...' because the necessary is that which is necessarily inherent in the soul of the creature in a way that it finds no way to separate from it, and necessity is the impossibility of separation, and they did not understand that what is meant by it is the impossibility of separation that is within power. His saying: 'For if its occurrence depended on knowledge of it, circularity would follow' — the circularity may be repelled by saying that the claim of al-Jahiz and his followers is that what the occurrence of obligation depends on is the affirmation of the possibility of obligation, which does not depend on occurrence but on possibility, for which occurrence is not a condition, so there is no circularity. And you are aware that applying the wording of the book to this, although possible by assuming an omitted genitive in his saying 'he does not know obligation', i.e., he does not know the possibility of obligation, yet his saying: 'because one who does not know these things does not know the possibility of obligation' would not be complete, because knowledge of the possibility of obligation does not depend on actual affirmation of the mentioned matters, but rather knowledge of the actual occurrence of obligation depends on it, so reflect. His saying: 'And it is refuted by what has passed of the testimony of inner sense' — for if you say: The testimony of inner sense and the discussion of circularity and regress only apply to reflection that needs reflection, not to what falls under it and other experiential, inner-sense, and other matters, so the refutation by the testimony and the necessity of circularity and regress on the supposition that all is theoretical, in the sense that appears from the argument, is not conceded. I say: Perhaps the Jahmiyya claimed that all is theoretical in the sense mentioned previously, opposite to necessary, imagining that necessary is that which the soul finds no way to separate from, and there is no knowledge except the soul is empty of it at the beginning of natural disposition. So they were refuted firstly by saying that their claim — I mean that all is theoretical in the well-known sense — is belied by inner sense, and secondly that their proof does not yield that. And it may be said: The dispute is verbal, and that what they mean by 'necessary' which they completely negated is that which does not depend on anything at all, and by 'theoretical' which they affirmed is that which depends on something in general, so ponder. — from them.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth weak doctrine (al-madhhab al-rabi') concerning whether knowledge is necessary or theoretical. The doctrine is attributed to the Jahmiyya, followers of Jahm ibn Safwan, who claim that all knowledge — both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq), whether required to believe or not — is theoretical (nazari). The author then refutes this doctrine with two arguments. First, he invokes the testimony of inner sense (shahadat al-wijdan), which clearly shows that some knowledge is necessary (daruri) and cannot be theoretical. Second, he argues that if all knowledge were theoretical, it would lead to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are absurd. Following the statement of the refutation, the commentary explains the meaning of 'yubtiluhu' (it is refuted) and clarifies that the refutation is within the defined scope of the dispute. It then elucidates the nature of necessary knowledge: that which the soul cannot separate itself from. The commentary adds that the Jahmiyya failed to understand that the relevant necessity is that which is within divine power. The next commentary addresses a potential objection to the circularity argument concerning the knowledge of obligation (taklif). The objection claims that circularity is avoided because what depends on occurrence is only the affirmation of possibility, not the occurrence itself. The commentator refutes this by showing that the argument's logic still holds. Finally, a further discussion considers the possibility that the entire dispute is merely verbal (lafzi), suggesting that the Jahmiyya might have used 'necessary' to mean 'that which depends on nothing at all' and 'theoretical' to mean 'that which depends on something in general'. The author leaves this for the reader to ponder.

Key Terms

ضروري
وجدان
جهمية
جبرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Jahmiyya claim applies only to certain types of knowledge, or that the refutation by wijdan is merely subjective.
Correction: The author insists that the claim is universal and that inner sense provides objective, undeniable evidence of necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint

In what areas of your faith have you trusted intellectual arguments more than the direct conviction Allah has placed in your heart? How can you realign your trust to give priority to that inner sense (wijdan)?
?
The passage describes necessary knowledge as that which the soul cannot separate from. Is there any truth about Allah or your faith that you have tried to push away or rationalize despite knowing it is undeniably true? What would it take for you to surrender to that necessary knowledge completely?

Refutation by Circularity and Regress
Presents the circularity/regress argument and discusses the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Jahmiyya claim that all knowledge is nazari leads to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), and how a potential objection regarding the knowledge of obligation is addressed.

Core Idea
If all knowledge were theoretical, it could never be founded, leading to circularity or regress.

This lesson continues the refutation of the Jahmiyya doctrine by presenting the second argument: that if all knowledge were theoretical, it would require an infinite chain of dependencies, resulting in either circularity or an endless regress. The author also discusses a possible counterargument concerning the knowledge of divine obligation and shows why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the circularity/regress argument
Read the passage starting from 'falaw tawaqqafa wuqu'uhu...' which presents the argument: if the occurrence of something depended on knowledge of it, circularity would arise.
The argument is concise; the commentary expands on it.
2
Examine the objection and its refutation
The commentary raises an objection from al-Jahiz that the dependence is only on the possibility of obligation, not its actual occurrence, thus no circularity. The author refutes this by showing that the knowledge of possibility itself depends on prior knowledge.
This shows the subtlety of the argument.
3
Reflect on the verbal dispute interpretation
The passage concludes with a suggestion that the whole dispute may be verbal: the Jahmiyya might have used 'daruri' to mean 'depends on nothing at all', making their claim trivial.
The author leaves this for pondering.

(المذهب الرابع) في هذه المسألة ( إن الكل نظري) سواء كان تصوراً أو تصديقاً مما يلزم اعتقاده أو لا يلزم ) وهو مذهب بعض الجهمية ) التابعين لجهم بن صفوان الترمذي رئيس الجبرية ويبطله) ما مر من شهادة الوجدان بكون البعض ضرورياً، و من لزوم الدور أو التسلسل على تقدير كون الكل نظرياً ) قوله : ( ويبطله ) أي كون الكل نظرياً بالمعنى الذي مر في تحرير محل النزاع، حيث قال : نقض مذاهب ضعيفة في هذه المسألة فإن فسر النظري بمعنى ما يتوقف على شيء كان خروجاً عن محل النزاع. قوله : ( بأن الضروري الخ لأن الضروري ما يلزم نفس المخلوق لزوماً لا يجد إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً ، واللزوم هو امتناع الانفكاك ولم يفهموا أن المراد منه امتناع الانفكاك المقدور. قوله : فلو) توقف وقوعه على العلم به لزم الدور) قد يدفع الدور بأن مدعى الجاحظ و متبعيه هو أن الموقوف عليه لوقوع التكليف هو التصديق بإمكان التكليف، وهو لا يتوقف على الوقوع بل الإمكان الذي ليس الوقوع شرطاً لثبوته فلا دور وأنت خبير بأن تطبيق عبارة الكتاب على هذا، وإن أمكن بحمله على حذف المضاف في قوله لا يعلم التكليف، أي لا يعلم إمكان التكليف إلا أنه لا يتم حينئذ قوله : لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور لا يعلم إمكان التكليف، لأن العلم بإمكان التكليف لا يتوقف على تحقق التصديق بالأمور المذكورة بالفعل، وإنما يتوقف عليه العلم بوقوع التكليف بالفعل فتدبر. قوله : ( ويبطله) ما مر من شهادة الوجدان فإن قلت : شهادة الوجدان وحديث الدور والتسلسل إنما كان في النظر الذي يحتاج إلى النظر لا فيما يتناوله وغيره من التجربيات والوجدانيات وغيرهما فالإبطال بالشهادة ولزوم الدور والتسلسل على تقدير كون الكل نظرياً، بالمعنى الذي يظهر من الاحتجاج ممنوع قلت : لعل الجهمية زعموا أن الكل نظري بالمعنى المذكور فيما سبق، المقابل للضروري توهماً أن الضروري ما لا تجد النفس إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلاً، وما من علم إلا والنفس خالية عنه في مبدأ الفطرة فرد عليهم أولاً بأن مدعاهم أعني نظرية الكل بالمعنى المشهور يكذبه الوجدان، وثانياً بأن دليلهم لا يفيد ذلك وقد يقال : النزاع لفظي وأن مرادهم بالضروري الذي نفوه بالكلية ما لا يتوقف على أمر أصلاً، وبالنظري الذي أثبتوه ما يتوقف على شيء في الجملة فتأمل . منهم
(The fourth madhhab) in this issue (is that all is theoretical [nazari]), whether it is a concept or an assent, whether it is necessary to believe or not. And it is the madhhab of some of the Jahmiyya, the followers of Jahm ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi, the head of the Jabriyya. And it is refuted by what has passed of the testimony of inner sense (shahadat al-wijdan) that some things are necessary (daruri), and by the necessity of circularity (dawr) or regress (tasalsul) on the supposition that all is theoretical. His saying: 'And it is refuted' means that all is theoretical in the sense that was mentioned in the clarification of the point of dispute, where he said: refuting weak doctrines in this issue, for if theoretical is interpreted as that which depends on something, it would be a departure from the point of dispute. His saying: 'Because the necessary (al-daruri) ...' because the necessary is that which is necessarily inherent in the soul of the creature in a way that it finds no way to separate from it, and necessity is the impossibility of separation, and they did not understand that what is meant by it is the impossibility of separation that is within power. His saying: 'For if its occurrence depended on knowledge of it, circularity would follow' — the circularity may be repelled by saying that the claim of al-Jahiz and his followers is that what the occurrence of obligation depends on is the affirmation of the possibility of obligation, which does not depend on occurrence but on possibility, for which occurrence is not a condition, so there is no circularity. And you are aware that applying the wording of the book to this, although possible by assuming an omitted genitive in his saying 'he does not know obligation', i.e., he does not know the possibility of obligation, yet his saying: 'because one who does not know these things does not know the possibility of obligation' would not be complete, because knowledge of the possibility of obligation does not depend on actual affirmation of the mentioned matters, but rather knowledge of the actual occurrence of obligation depends on it, so reflect. His saying: 'And it is refuted by what has passed of the testimony of inner sense' — for if you say: The testimony of inner sense and the discussion of circularity and regress only apply to reflection that needs reflection, not to what falls under it and other experiential, inner-sense, and other matters, so the refutation by the testimony and the necessity of circularity and regress on the supposition that all is theoretical, in the sense that appears from the argument, is not conceded. I say: Perhaps the Jahmiyya claimed that all is theoretical in the sense mentioned previously, opposite to necessary, imagining that necessary is that which the soul finds no way to separate from, and there is no knowledge except the soul is empty of it at the beginning of natural disposition. So they were refuted firstly by saying that their claim — I mean that all is theoretical in the well-known sense — is belied by inner sense, and secondly that their proof does not yield that. And it may be said: The dispute is verbal, and that what they mean by 'necessary' which they completely negated is that which does not depend on anything at all, and by 'theoretical' which they affirmed is that which depends on something in general, so ponder. — from them.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth weak doctrine (al-madhhab al-rabi') concerning whether knowledge is necessary or theoretical. The doctrine is attributed to the Jahmiyya, followers of Jahm ibn Safwan, who claim that all knowledge — both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq), whether required to believe or not — is theoretical (nazari). The author then refutes this doctrine with two arguments. First, he invokes the testimony of inner sense (shahadat al-wijdan), which clearly shows that some knowledge is necessary (daruri) and cannot be theoretical. Second, he argues that if all knowledge were theoretical, it would lead to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are absurd. Following the statement of the refutation, the commentary explains the meaning of 'yubtiluhu' (it is refuted) and clarifies that the refutation is within the defined scope of the dispute. It then elucidates the nature of necessary knowledge: that which the soul cannot separate itself from. The commentary adds that the Jahmiyya failed to understand that the relevant necessity is that which is within divine power. The next commentary addresses a potential objection to the circularity argument concerning the knowledge of obligation (taklif). The objection claims that circularity is avoided because what depends on occurrence is only the affirmation of possibility, not the occurrence itself. The commentator refutes this by showing that the argument's logic still holds. Finally, a further discussion considers the possibility that the entire dispute is merely verbal (lafzi), suggesting that the Jahmiyya might have used 'necessary' to mean 'that which depends on nothing at all' and 'theoretical' to mean 'that which depends on something in general'. The author leaves this for the reader to ponder.

?

?
This lesson shows that the attempt to make all knowledge dependent on proof leads to an impossible regress. Jahmiyya's Proof That All Knowledge is Theoretical
The Jahmiyya proof that all knowledge is nazari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jahmiyya's argument that all knowledge must be theoretical (nazari) because the soul begins empty and acquires knowledge gradually.

Core Idea
The soul's initial emptiness is used to argue that no knowledge is necessary.

The Jahmiyya school advanced a powerful argument to support their view that all knowledge is attained through reflection and experience, not given innately. Their proof hinges on the observation that the human soul is born without knowledge and only later acquires it through conditions like sensation and report. This lesson presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a proof, not a definition.
2
Identify the key premise
The Jahmiyya start by stating that necessary knowledge cannot be absent from the soul. Then they observe that at the beginning of natural disposition (fitra), the soul is empty of all knowledge.
This is the crucial observation that leads to their conclusion.
3
Follow the conclusion
Since knowledge is acquired gradually through conditions, it cannot be necessary in the sense of being always present. Therefore, all knowledge must be theoretical (nazari).
The argument seems straightforward, but note the ambiguity in 'necessary'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا) على مذهبهم ( بأن الضروري يمنع خلو النفس عنه، وما من علم) تصوري أو تصديقي ( إلا والنفس خالية عنه في مبدأ الفطرة، ثم تحصل لها علومها ( بالتدريج بحسب ما يتفق من الشروط كالإحساس والتجربة والتواتر ،وغيرها فيكون الكل غير ضروري وهو المراد بالنظري ) والجواب أن الضروري) المقابل للنظري ( قد تخلو عنه النفس إما عند من يوقفه ( كالمعتزلة والفلاسفة ) على (شرط كالتوجه والإحساس وغيرهما أو استعداد) به تقبل النفس ذلك العلم الضروري ( فلفقده ) أي فقد ذلك الموقوف عليه من الشرط والاستعداد ( وأما عندنا ) يعني القائلين باستناد الأشياء كلها إلى اختياره تعالى ابتداء ( فإذ قد لا يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد ( حيناً ثم يخلقه فيه بلا قدرة من العبد متعلقة بذلك العلم ( أو نظر) منه يترتب عليه ذلك العلم عادة فيكون ضرورياً غير مقدور إذ لم تتعلق به قدرة العبد ابتداءً، ولا بواسطة. ) قوله : ( أن الضروري المقابل للنظري) أي الضروري بالمعنى الأعم حاله ما ذكر، لأن الضروري وإن كان بالمعنى الذي يرادف البديهي أيضاً يمكن توقفه على شرط، وليس هذا لقيد احترازاً عن الضروري المقابل للكسبي إذ لا فائدة فيه لتلازمهما في الوجود عادة كما مر، بل إشارةً إلى تعليل جواز الخلو وهو أن الضروري المقابل للنظري إنما يقتضي عدم توقفه على النظر لا امتناع الخلو عنه، وإلى أن خلاصة الجواب يرجع إلى الترديد وهو أنه إن أريد بالضروري ما ليس فلا نسلم امتناع الخلو عنه، وإن أريد به معنى آخر فهو لا يقابل النظري فلا يلزم من كون العلوم ضرورية بذلك المعنى كونها نظرية بالمعنى المتنازع فيه فلا يصح قوله : فيكون الكل غير ضروري وهو المراد بالنظري.
And they argued (ihtajju) for their doctrine (that the necessary prevents the soul from being empty of it, and there is no knowledge) – whether concept or assent – (except that the soul is empty of it at the beginning of natural disposition, then its sciences are acquired gradually according to the conditions that occur, such as sensation, experience, report (tawatur), and others, so all is not necessary (daruri), and this is what is meant by theoretical (nazari). And the answer is that the necessary (al-daruri) opposite to theoretical (al-nazari) (may be absent from the soul, either according to those who make it dependent – like the Mu'tazila and philosophers – on a condition such as attention, sensation, etc., or on a preparedness (isti'dad) by which the soul receives that necessary knowledge, so due to the absence of that which it depends on, i.e., the loss of that condition or preparedness; or according to us – meaning those who hold that all things are grounded in His choice (ikhtiyar) initially – for He may not create it in the servant at one time, then create it in him without any power of the servant related to that knowledge, or without reflection (nazar) from him that would ordinarily lead to that knowledge, so it is necessary (daruri) and not within power, since the servant's power does not pertain to it initially or intermediately.) His saying: 'That the necessary opposite to theoretical' – i.e., the necessary in the broader sense is as mentioned, because the necessary, even in the sense synonymous with the self-evident (badihi), can depend on a condition. This qualification is not to distinguish it from the necessary opposite to the acquired (al-kasbi), for there is no benefit in that due to their mutual concomitance in existence usually as has passed, but rather it is an indication to explain the permissibility of absence, and that the necessary opposite to theoretical only requires its not depending on reflection, not the impossibility of its absence. And that the gist of the answer reduces to a disjunction: if by 'necessary' is meant that which is not [theoretical?], we do not concede the impossibility of its absence; and if another meaning is intended, then it is not opposed to theoretical, so it does not follow from sciences being necessary in that sense that they are theoretical in the disputed sense, so his saying: 'so all is not necessary, and that is what is meant by theoretical' is not valid.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the Jahmiyya's own proof (ihtijaj) in support of their doctrine that all knowledge is theoretical. They argue that the nature of necessary knowledge (daruri) is that it cannot be absent from the soul; yet, at the beginning of human natural disposition (fitra), the soul is empty of all knowledge, which is then acquired gradually through conditions like sensation, experience, and recurrent reports (tawatur). Therefore, they conclude, no knowledge is necessary, and all is theoretical. The answer to this proof is then given. It explains that necessary knowledge (in the sense opposite to theoretical) can indeed be absent from the soul. This can happen in two ways: either because the necessary knowledge depends on conditions (such as attention or sensation) or on a preparedness (isti'dad) that may be absent – as held by the Mu'tazila and philosophers; or, according to the Ash'ari position (mentioned as 'us'), because God may simply not create that necessary knowledge in a person at a given time, and later create it without any power or reflective effort on the servant's part, making it necessary (daruri) but not within the servant's capacity. The commentary expands on this by clarifying that the necessary here is the one opposed to theoretical (nazari), which only means it does not depend on reflection (nazar), not that it cannot be absent. The conclusion is that the Jahmiyya's argument fails because they equivocate on the meaning of 'necessary'. If by 'necessary' they mean that which cannot be absent, then it is not conceded that knowledge cannot be absent (since even necessary knowledge can be absent due to missing conditions or divine will). If they mean something else, then it is not the same necessary that is opposite to theoretical, so the inference that all knowledge is theoretical does not follow. Thus, the refutation is complete.

Key Terms
احتجاج
ضروري
نظري
فطرة
شروط
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Jahmiyya argument, why must all knowledge be theoretical?
A) Because necessary knowledge cannot be acquired gradually.
B) Because the soul is empty of all knowledge at the beginning and later acquires it.
C) Because all knowledge is due to sensation.
D) Because theoretical knowledge is the opposite of necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Jahmiyya argue that since the soul starts empty and acquires knowledge gradually, no knowledge can be necessary, so all is theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that all your religious knowledge is theoretical and acquired through conditions, how seriously do you verify the sources of that knowledge? Are you certain the reports you rely on are reliable?
?
The Jahmiyya argue that you start with an empty soul. Does that make you feel a burden to actively seek knowledge? What have you done today to fill that emptiness with sound knowledge of your Creator?
?
You may assume some truths about Allah are 'obvious' and need no effort. But this passage challenges that assumption. If no knowledge is necessary, then what excuse do you have for not pursuing the conditions that bring knowledge?

The Response: Why Necessary Knowledge Can Be Absent
The response to the Jahmiyya proof and its logical clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the Jahmiyya proof, which shows that necessary knowledge can be absent due to missing conditions or because God does not create it.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is not that which is always present; its absence is compatible with its necessity.

The Jahmiyya argument seems compelling, but it relies on a flawed understanding of what necessary knowledge means. The response clarifies that 'necessary' only indicates independence from reflection, not continuous presence. The absence of knowledge from the soul at the start does not disprove its potential necessity when conditions are met or when God wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the answer begins by stating that necessary knowledge can be absent.
This directly challenges the Jahmiyya's premise.
2
Identify the two explanations
The answer gives two reasons why necessary knowledge can be absent: 1) it depends on conditions (Mu'tazila/philosophers), 2) God may not create it (Ash'ari).
Each explanation shows that absence does not contradict necessity.
3
Understand the logical clarification
The commentary explains that 'necessary' opposite to 'theoretical' only means it does not depend on reflection. It does not mean it cannot be absent. This resolves the ambiguity.
The Jahmiyya equivocated on 'necessary'.

واحتجوا) على مذهبهم ( بأن الضروري يمنع خلو النفس عنه، وما من علم) تصوري أو تصديقي ( إلا والنفس خالية عنه في مبدأ الفطرة، ثم تحصل لها علومها ( بالتدريج بحسب ما يتفق من الشروط كالإحساس والتجربة والتواتر ،وغيرها فيكون الكل غير ضروري وهو المراد بالنظري ) والجواب أن الضروري) المقابل للنظري ( قد تخلو عنه النفس إما عند من يوقفه ( كالمعتزلة والفلاسفة ) على (شرط كالتوجه والإحساس وغيرهما أو استعداد) به تقبل النفس ذلك العلم الضروري ( فلفقده ) أي فقد ذلك الموقوف عليه من الشرط والاستعداد ( وأما عندنا ) يعني القائلين باستناد الأشياء كلها إلى اختياره تعالى ابتداء ( فإذ قد لا يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد ( حيناً ثم يخلقه فيه بلا قدرة من العبد متعلقة بذلك العلم ( أو نظر) منه يترتب عليه ذلك العلم عادة فيكون ضرورياً غير مقدور إذ لم تتعلق به قدرة العبد ابتداءً، ولا بواسطة. ) قوله : ( أن الضروري المقابل للنظري) أي الضروري بالمعنى الأعم حاله ما ذكر، لأن الضروري وإن كان بالمعنى الذي يرادف البديهي أيضاً يمكن توقفه على شرط، وليس هذا لقيد احترازاً عن الضروري المقابل للكسبي إذ لا فائدة فيه لتلازمهما في الوجود عادة كما مر، بل إشارةً إلى تعليل جواز الخلو وهو أن الضروري المقابل للنظري إنما يقتضي عدم توقفه على النظر لا امتناع الخلو عنه، وإلى أن خلاصة الجواب يرجع إلى الترديد وهو أنه إن أريد بالضروري ما ليس فلا نسلم امتناع الخلو عنه، وإن أريد به معنى آخر فهو لا يقابل النظري فلا يلزم من كون العلوم ضرورية بذلك المعنى كونها نظرية بالمعنى المتنازع فيه فلا يصح قوله : فيكون الكل غير ضروري وهو المراد بالنظري.
And they argued (ihtajju) for their doctrine (that the necessary prevents the soul from being empty of it, and there is no knowledge) – whether concept or assent – (except that the soul is empty of it at the beginning of natural disposition, then its sciences are acquired gradually according to the conditions that occur, such as sensation, experience, report (tawatur), and others, so all is not necessary (daruri), and this is what is meant by theoretical (nazari). And the answer is that the necessary (al-daruri) opposite to theoretical (al-nazari) (may be absent from the soul, either according to those who make it dependent – like the Mu'tazila and philosophers – on a condition such as attention, sensation, etc., or on a preparedness (isti'dad) by which the soul receives that necessary knowledge, so due to the absence of that which it depends on, i.e., the loss of that condition or preparedness; or according to us – meaning those who hold that all things are grounded in His choice (ikhtiyar) initially – for He may not create it in the servant at one time, then create it in him without any power of the servant related to that knowledge, or without reflection (nazar) from him that would ordinarily lead to that knowledge, so it is necessary (daruri) and not within power, since the servant's power does not pertain to it initially or intermediately.) His saying: 'That the necessary opposite to theoretical' – i.e., the necessary in the broader sense is as mentioned, because the necessary, even in the sense synonymous with the self-evident (badihi), can depend on a condition. This qualification is not to distinguish it from the necessary opposite to the acquired (al-kasbi), for there is no benefit in that due to their mutual concomitance in existence usually as has passed, but rather it is an indication to explain the permissibility of absence, and that the necessary opposite to theoretical only requires its not depending on reflection, not the impossibility of its absence. And that the gist of the answer reduces to a disjunction: if by 'necessary' is meant that which is not [theoretical?], we do not concede the impossibility of its absence; and if another meaning is intended, then it is not opposed to theoretical, so it does not follow from sciences being necessary in that sense that they are theoretical in the disputed sense, so his saying: 'so all is not necessary, and that is what is meant by theoretical' is not valid.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the Jahmiyya's own proof (ihtijaj) in support of their doctrine that all knowledge is theoretical. They argue that the nature of necessary knowledge (daruri) is that it cannot be absent from the soul; yet, at the beginning of human natural disposition (fitra), the soul is empty of all knowledge, which is then acquired gradually through conditions like sensation, experience, and recurrent reports (tawatur). Therefore, they conclude, no knowledge is necessary, and all is theoretical. The answer to this proof is then given. It explains that necessary knowledge (in the sense opposite to theoretical) can indeed be absent from the soul. This can happen in two ways: either because the necessary knowledge depends on conditions (such as attention or sensation) or on a preparedness (isti'dad) that may be absent – as held by the Mu'tazila and philosophers; or, according to the Ash'ari position (mentioned as 'us'), because God may simply not create that necessary knowledge in a person at a given time, and later create it without any power or reflective effort on the servant's part, making it necessary (daruri) but not within the servant's capacity. The commentary expands on this by clarifying that the necessary here is the one opposed to theoretical (nazari), which only means it does not depend on reflection (nazar), not that it cannot be absent. The conclusion is that the Jahmiyya's argument fails because they equivocate on the meaning of 'necessary'. If by 'necessary' they mean that which cannot be absent, then it is not conceded that knowledge cannot be absent (since even necessary knowledge can be absent due to missing conditions or divine will). If they mean something else, then it is not the same necessary that is opposite to theoretical, so the inference that all knowledge is theoretical does not follow. Thus, the refutation is complete.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
شرط
استعداد
اختيار
ترديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge cannot be absent from the soul.
Correction: Necessary knowledge in the kalam sense only means it does not depend on reflection (nazar); it can be absent due to missing conditions or divine will.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why can necessary knowledge be absent from the soul?
A) Because it is not really necessary.
B) Because it depends on conditions that may be absent or because God may not create it.
C) Because the soul is always empty.
D) Because theoretical knowledge is more important.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge can be absent because it depends on conditions or on God's creation; this refutes the Jahmiyya's claim that all knowledge is theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lose certainty about a belief you once held as self-evident, do you attribute that loss to a flaw in yourself or to a deeper wisdom beyond your control? This lesson teaches that necessary knowledge can be absent for reasons beyond your power—how does that reshape your patience in seeking truth?
?
If knowledge of Allah and His attributes were always within your grasp, would you be more grateful for them? But they are not; they come at His will. How often do you thank Him for the very act of knowing something about Him, rather than just the content of that knowledge?
?
The Jahmiyya assumed that if knowledge is necessary, it cannot be absent, and thus concluded that all knowledge is theoretical. Is there a 'Jahmiyya' inside you—an assumption that because something should be evident, its absence proves it is not real? How does this lesson call you to humbly accept that your intellect does not dictate when truth appears?

Clarifying the Meaning of 'Necessary' in the Objection
Commentator disambiguates 'necessary' to refute an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's response to the objection about the temporary absence of necessary knowledge by distinguishing two meanings of 'necessary'.

Core Idea
The objection confuses two meanings of 'necessary'; the correct meaning allows temporary absence.

The objection claimed that if necessary knowledge can be absent, then all knowledge becomes theoretical. The commentator responds by disambiguating the term 'necessary'. He shows that the objection only works if we conflate the necessary as opposite of theoretical with another meaning. This passage introduces a classic dialectical move: clarifying definitions before evaluating an argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the full literal translation. Let the commentator's own phrasing sink in before analyzing.
The ending 'binẓarī intifā' might be corrupt; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'necessary'
The commentator distinguishes between the necessary as opposite of theoretical (al-ḍarūrī al-muqābil lil-naẓarī) and necessary in a broader sense. Find where he discusses these.
The broader sense can sometimes depend on a condition and may be absent.
3
Understand the disjunctive response
The answer is a disjunction (tardīd): if by 'necessary' the opponent means the opposite of theoretical, then we can deny that it cannot be absent. If they mean another sense, then it does not contrast with theoretical, so the conclusion fails.
This is the core logical move.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why clarifying terms is essential in theological debates. This passage shows how a simple ambiguity can lead to a faulty argument.
This connects to broader kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'the necessary (al-ḍarūrī) as opposed to the theoretical (al-naẓarī)' means the necessary in the broader sense as previously mentioned, because the necessary, even if it is synonymous with the intuitive (al-badīhī), can also depend on a condition. This qualification is not to avoid the necessary as opposed to the acquired (al-kasbī) since there is no benefit in that due to their usual coexistence as previously, but rather an indication of the explanation of the permissibility of [temporary] absence, which is that the necessary as opposed to the theoretical only requires that it does not depend on reflection (naẓar), not the impossibility of being free from it. And that the gist of the answer reduces to a disjunction: if by 'necessary' is meant that which is not [theoretical], we do not grant the impossibility of being free from it; and if another meaning is intended, then it does not contrast with theoretical, so it does not follow that sciences being necessary in that sense are theoretical in the disputed sense, so his statement 'then all would be non-necessary, which is what is meant by theoretical' is invalid. Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a response to an objection. The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning of 'the necessary as opposed to the theoretical'. He states that this necessary is the broader sense (al-a'amm) that can sometimes depend on a condition, not the necessary as opposed to acquired knowledge. The point is to explain why it is permissible for the soul to sometimes lack this necessary knowledge. The answer is a disjunction: if the opponent means by 'necessary' what is not theoretical, then we deny that it cannot be absent; if he means something else, then it does not contrast with theoretical, so the conclusion that all knowledge would be theoretical does not follow. The last line 'binẓarī intifā' is unclear but likely part of the original text.

Limitations of Introspective Sciences and Inclusion of Intuitive Sciences
Commentary on limitations of introspective knowledge and inclusion of intuitive sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why introspective sciences (wijdāniyyāt) have limited utility in argumentation and why intuitive sciences (ḥadsiyyāt) are included under sensory sciences (ḥissiyyāt).

Core Idea
Introspective knowledge is private and rare; intuitive knowledge is sensory-based but debatable.

This passage from the commentary delves deeper into the divisions of knowledge introduced earlier. It clarifies a potential misunderstanding about introspective sciences: they are not entirely private, but their shared aspects are too rare to serve as universal proofs. Then, it addresses the classification of intuitive sciences, explaining why the author includes them under sensory sciences despite some debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the limitation of wijdāniyyāt
Read the commentary's explanation that introspective sciences are of little benefit because they are only known to the experiencer. Note the nuance: the lack of knowledge about sharing does not mean they are not shared; but even when shared, it is rare.
The author uses the example of self-existence as a rare shared introspective knowledge.
2
Grasp the inclusion of ḥadsiyyāt
Focus on the commentator's statement that ḥadsiyyāt are included under ḥissiyyāt. Understand the basis for this inclusion as mentioned in the text.
The text is incomplete here, but the commentator notes it will be clarified later.
3
Appreciate the need to prove types of necessary sciences
See why it is necessary to prove the types of necessary sciences (ḍarūriyyāt) rather than just their absolute existence. Different inquiries rely on different types, so a general proof is insufficient.
This is in the middle part about 'in proving the necessary sciences'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإنها قليلة (النفع لإفادتها العلم لصاحب الوجدان . قوله : أي غير معلومة (الاشتراك صرف المتن عن ظاهره لأن غاية الأمر عدم العلم بالاشتراك لا انتفاء الاشتراك ثم عدم العلم أيضاً أكثري، وإلا فبعض الوجدانيات معلوم الاشتراك كعلمنا بوجود ذواتنا، ولذا يستدل بالوجدان في بعض المطالب ولقلته وندرته لم يصح بكونها حجة عند اشتراك الوجدان، وأشار إليه بكلمة ربما، وقال المصنف : إنها قليلة النفع لقلة مواد اشتراكها . قوله : ( والحدسيات) أدرجها في الحسيات إما بناء على ما سيصرح به فيما بعد من أنه لا ... قوله : ( في إثبات العلوم (الضرورية أي إثبات أنواعها ولا بد من هذا الإثبات، لأن بعض المطالب منته إلى بعض معين منها، والبعض الآخر إلى البعض الآخر، فلا يكفي إثبات مطلقها بل يحتاج إلى إثبات أقسامه ليصح الاحتجاج في مطلوب مطلوب فلا يرد أن هذا قد علم مما سبق من حيث بين أن الكل ليس بنظري على أن الإثبات يجوز أن يكون من الثبات، وهو إنما يحصل بدفع شبهة الخصم. قوله : ( والحدسيات قيل: إدراج الحدسيات في الحسيات التي للحس الظاهر مدخل فيها محل بحث، لأن بعض الحدسيات بالنسبة إلى بعض الأشخاص نظري بالنسبة إلى آخر
His statement: 'and that they are of little benefit' (meaning: because they only give knowledge to the one who experiences them. His statement: 'i.e., not known to be shared' is a reinterpretation of the text from its apparent meaning, because the most that can be said is that there is no knowledge of sharing, not the negation of sharing. Moreover, ignorance is also common. Otherwise, some introspective knowledge is known to be shared, such as our knowledge of our own existence. Therefore, introspection is used as evidence in some inquiries. But due to its scarcity and rarity, it is not valid as a proof when sharing is required. The author alluded to this with the word 'perhaps'. And the author said: they are of little benefit because of the scarcity of matters in which they are shared. His statement: 'and the intuitive (al-ḥadsiyyāt)' – he included them among the sensory sciences, either based on what he will later clarify, that there is no ... His statement: 'in proving the necessary sciences' i.e., proving their types. This proof is necessary because some inquiries end with a specific type of them, and others with another type. So proving them in absolute terms is not sufficient; rather, one needs to prove their divisions so that argumentation can be sound for each inquiry. It cannot be objected that this was already known from what preceded, where it was shown that not all is theoretical, but the proof can be one of stability (thubāt), which is only achieved by refuting the opponent's doubt. His statement: 'and the intuitive (al-ḥadsiyyāt)' – it is said: the inclusion of the intuitive among the sensory sciences in which the external sense has a role is debatable, because some intuitive sciences are theoretical for some people and [something else for others?]...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the earlier divisions. First, it clarifies that introspective sciences (wijdāniyyāt) are only known to the individual, but this does not mean they are not shared; rather, we simply do not know if others share them. Some, like self-knowledge, are universally shared, but they are rare. Therefore, they are of little benefit as proof for others. The commentator then addresses the inclusion of intuitive sciences (ḥadsiyyāt) under sensory sciences (ḥissiyyāt). He notes that the author includes them based on a later clarification. Finally, the commentator explains why it is necessary to prove the types of necessary sciences: different inquiries depend on different types, so a general proof is insufficient. The last part notes a debate about whether intuitive sciences should be classified under sensory sciences, as some intuitive sciences are theoretical for some people.

Key Terms
الوجدانيات (al-wijdāniyyāt)
الاشتراك (al-ishtirāk)
الحدسيات (al-ḥadsiyyāt)
الحسيات (al-ḥissiyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all introspective knowledge is entirely private and cannot be shared.
Correction: Some introspective knowledge, like the knowledge of one's own existence, is universally shared. However, such instances are too rare to function as a general proof.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that some introspective knowledge is 'known to be shared' (معلوم الاشتراك)?
A) Because it is based on sense perception.
B) Because everyone knows their own existence.
C) Because it is theoretical.
D) Because it is divine revelation.

Key Takeaway
Introspective knowledge is rarely shared and thus a weak proof; intuitive sciences are classified under sensory sciences despite debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your private spiritual experiences are universally shared and can be used as proof for others? Can you recall a moment when you insisted on a personal feeling as evidence? What would the author's argument about the rarity of shared introspection demand of you in that situation?
?
Consider a deep conviction about tawḥīd that you think every person should intuitively feel. Are you willing to accept that this conviction may not be universally shared and cannot be used as a general proof? How would that change the way you invite others to Islam?
?
When you experience a strong intuition about a religious truth, how do you determine whether it is from Allah or merely a personal guess? What accountability do you have to test that intuition against shared knowledge? Will you commit to not using private intuitions as evidence in discussions with others?

The Two Pillars of Proof: Sensed Things and Self-Evidents
Al-Jurjani enumerates the two main categories of necessary sciences and their proof conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that self-evidents are absolutely proof, while sensed things are proof only when their causes (experience, recurrent report, intuition, observation) are shared.

Core Idea
Sensed things and self-evidents are the mainstays of all sciences, but their proof value differs.

Al-Jurjani begins by identifying the two foundational categories of necessary knowledge. Understanding their characteristics is essential for evaluating how different types of evidence function in theological and philosophical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the clear division between the two categories.
The passage is a single concise statement defining the two categories and their conditions.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two terms: 'sensed things' (الحسيات) and 'self-evidents' (البديهيات). Note what is said about each.
Self-evidents are described as 'absolutely' proof, while sensed things require shared causes.
3
Understand the condition for sensed things
The condition is 'when the sharing in their causes is established'. Ask yourself why this condition is necessary for proof.
This connects to the idea that empirical knowledge is only persuasive if others share the same sensory experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي الأوليات وما في حكمها من القضايا الفطرية القياس فهذان القسمان أعني الحسيات والبديهيات هما العمدة في العلوم وهما يقومان حجة على الغير، أما البديهيات فعلى الإطلاق وأما الحسيات فإذا ثبت الاشتراك في أسبابها أعني فيما يقتضيها من تجربة أو تواتر أو حدس أو مشاهدة ) والناس فيهما فرق أربع حسب بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي الحاصل بلا تجشم كسب إلا أنه لما كان التعويل فيها على ذلك القياس علماً يحصل للنفس بمجرد القياس الحدسي من غير استعانة بالحس منها، كمالاً لصاحب النفس القدسية، وإما بناء على أن المراد بما للحس مدخل فيها أعم من مدخليته في جميع أنواعها أو بعضها . قوله : ( أو مشاهدة ) أي إدراك بإحدى الحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة، ليعم الوهميات وهذا هو الحق، فإن مشاهداتك ليست حجة على ما لم يكن له ذلك المشعر والشعور، وإنما ترك هذا من النظريات إنما تحصل من مقدمات لا دخل للحس فيها وبالجملة من له قوة قدسية يحصل له العلوم بالحدس مع أنه لا دخل للحس في بعض العلوم قطعاً، والجواب أولاً أن الكلام في الضروريات العامة ولا حدس للعامة إلا ويتقدم الحس عليه مثل الحكم بأن نور القمر مستفاد الشمس، وثانياً أن ما أدخلناه في الحسيات من الحدسيات هو الذي للحس مدخل فيه، والبعض الآخر مندرج في البديهيات لأنه في حكم الأوليات كالقضايا الفطرية القياس في أن العقل لا يحتاج إلى نظر فتأمل . قوله : (أي الأوليات) وجه التفسير أن البديهي بمعنى الضروري يعم الكل، وكون فطرية القياس في حكم الأوليات بناء على أن الوسط لما لم يفارق تصور الطرفين فكأنه لا احتياج هناك للعقل إلا إلى تصور الطرفين. قطعاً،
i.e., the first principles and what is in their status among the innate propositions of syllogism. So these two divisions, namely the sensed things and the self-evidents, are the mainstays in the sciences, and they constitute a proof against another. As for the self-evidents, absolutely; and as for the sensed things, when the sharing in their causes is established, i.e., in what necessitates them from experience, recurrent report, intuitive inference, or observation. And people regarding them are four groups according to the beginning in the intuitive inferences from repetition of observation and comparison of the hidden syllogism that occurs without burdening acquisition, except that because reliance in them is on that syllogism, knowledge obtains for the soul merely by the intuitive syllogism without the aid of sense from it, as a perfection for the possessor of the holy soul. Or based on the understanding that what is meant by 'sensation has a part in them' is more general than its having a part in all of their types or some of them.
His saying: 'or observation': i.e., perception by one of the external or internal senses, to include the estimations (al-wahmiyyāt), and this is the truth. For your observations are not a proof against one who does not have that sense and feeling. And rather this was left from the theoretical matters that are only obtained from premises in which sensation has no part. In summary, one who has a holy soul obtains sciences by intuition (al-ḥads) even though sensation has no part in some sciences, absolutely. The answer: first, that the discourse is concerning the general necessary sciences, and there is no intuition for the common people except that sensation precedes it, such as the judgment that the light of the moon is borrowed from the sun. Second, that what we included in the sensed things from the intuitive inferences is that in which sensation has a part, and the other part is included in the self-evidents because it is in the status of first principles, like the innate propositions of syllogism, in that the intellect does not need reflection. So reflect.
His saying: 'i.e., the first principles'. The explanation is that 'self-evident' in the sense of 'necessary' encompasses all. And the fact that the innate propositions of syllogism are in the status of first principles is based on that, because since the middle term does not separate from the conception of the two extremes, it is as if there is no need for the intellect except to conceive the two extremes. Absolutely.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and defense of the classification of necessary sciences, focusing on intuitive inferences (حدسيات) and their relation to sensed things (حسيات) and self-evidents (بديهيات). It states that الحسيات and البديهيات are the main proofs, with البديهيات being absolutely valid, while الحسيات are valid only when their causes (experience, recurrent report, intuition, observation) are shared. The commentator then addresses the inclusion of الحدسيات in الحسيات, noting two possible reasons: either because they involve a hidden syllogism that occurs without effort (perfection of the holy soul), or because 'sensation has a part' is interpreted broadly. An objection is raised that some الحدسيات are purely intellectual, to which the answer is twofold: first, the discussion is about general necessary sciences, and common people's intuition depends on prior sensation; second, those حدسيات that involve sensation are included in الحسيات, while those that do not are incorporated into البديهيات, like innate propositions of syllogism. Finally, the commentator explains that 'self-evident' in the sense of 'necessary' includes all, and that فطرية القياس (innate propositions of syllogism) are in the status of first principles because the middle term does not separate from the extremes.

الحسيات
البديهيات
الأوليات
الفطرية القياس
تجربة
تواتر
حدس
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensed things are always proof for everyone, just like self-evidents.
Correction: Sensed things are only proof when their causes (the experiences that produced them) are shared; otherwise they are not binding on others.

Checkpoint
According to the text, when do sensed things (حسيات) constitute a proof against another person?
A) When the sensation itself is shared
B) When their causes (experience, recurrent report, intuition, observation) are shared
C) When they are intuitive
D) When they are observed

Key Takeaway
Self-evidents are proof absolutely, while sensed things require that their causes (experience, tawātur, intuition, observation) are shared with others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one belief you hold about a religious matter that you consider 'self-evident.' Is it truly innate and undeniable, or have you simply never questioned its foundation? What would happen if you treated it as a 'sensed thing' and examined whether its causes are shared with others? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to test that belief.
?
You often rely on reports from scholars, family, or community to accept truths about the unseen. Al-Jurjani says sensed things are only proof when their causes (like experience or recurrent report) are shared. Are you personally convinced of the reliability of those causes, or do you accept them passively? Identify one area where your faith rests on hearsay and commit to verifying it through tawātur or firsthand investigation.
?
When you share your faith with others, do you treat your personal experiences as proof? According to this lesson, they are only valid when the causes are shared. How can you ensure you are not demanding others accept your subjective experiences as evidence? End this reflection with a practical change in how you present your beliefs to others.

The Inclusion of Intuitive Inferences in Sensed Things
Al-Jurjani explains the inclusion of intuitive inferences in sensed things and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intuitive inferences are considered part of sensed things and how the objection regarding purely intellectual intuition is answered.

Core Idea
Intuitive inferences involve a hidden syllogism but are based on prior sense data, hence classified as sensed things.

The text now delves into a subtlety: intuitive inferences (ḥadsiyyāt) seem purely rational, yet they are classified under sensed things. Al-Jurjani explains two reasons and answers an objection concerning sciences attained by a holy soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe the two reasons given for including intuitive inferences in sensed things.
The passage contains a structured argument with reasons and an objection-response.
2
Identify the two reasons
Look for the two reasons: (1) because intuitive knowledge relies on a hidden syllogism from repeated observations, and (2) because 'sensation has a part' is interpreted broadly.
The first reason emphasizes the hidden syllogism; the second broadens the definition.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection is about the holy soul obtaining sciences purely by intuition. Find how the commentator responds in two parts.
The response distinguishes general necessary sciences (where sensation precedes) and reclassifies pure intuitions as self-evidents.

?

When you try to convince others of a truth, do you rely more on your own sensory evidence or on rational arguments that could be universally grasped? What does that reveal about your approach to da'wah or dialogue?

Defining Observation and the Status of Innate Syllogisms
Al-Jurjani defines observation and explains the status of innate syllogistic propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that observation includes internal senses, and that innate syllogistic propositions are self-evident because their middle term is inseparable from the extremes.

Core Idea
Observation is both external and internal; innate syllogisms are self-evident due to the implicit middle term.

Al-Jurjani now clarifies two final points: the scope of 'observation' used in the causes of sensed things, and why innate propositions of syllogism (fiṭriyyāt qiyāsiyya) are considered first principles. These remarks complete the classification of necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that it consists of two separate comments: one on 'observation' and one on 'first principles'.
Both comments are short but significant for the overall classification.
2
Understand the definition of observation
Observation is defined as perception by external or internal senses, including estimative judgments (wahmiyyāt). Consider why this broad definition is important.
It ensures that observations are not limited to the five senses but include inner perceptions.
3
Grasp why innate syllogisms are like first principles
The reason is that the middle term does not separate from the concepts of the subject and predicate. Ask yourself: Does this make them self-evident?
The intellect only needs to conceive the extremes; the middle term is implicitly known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
