Objection and Resolution on Shared Inner Experiences
Al-Jurjani addresses an objection about inner experiences as proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection raised against the claim that inner experiences are not shared proofs and the resolution offered by distinguishing between two interpretations of 'not shared'.

Core Idea
Inner experiences are inherently private, not just unknown to be shared.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary tackles a subtle objection: if sharing in causes makes other sensory data proof, why don't inner experiences also become proof when their sharing is established? The answer hinges on how we interpret 'not shared'—as a factual impossibility or mere lack of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector points out a tension: if the commentator allows other divisions to be proofs when sharing in causes is established, why not inner experiences?
The objector assumes that sharing in inner experiences could be known.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
The passage presents two ways to understand 'inner experiences are not shared': (a) they are not known to be shared with certainty (the commentator's interpretation), or (b) they are not actually shared in reality. See how each leads to a different conclusion.
The literal interpretation (b) is defended by the Imām and the author.
3
Understand why the literal interpretation resolves the objection
If inner experiences are inherently private, then even if sharing in causes is established, they cannot be shared. Thus the objection disappears because the scenario 'if sharing is established' is impossible. The resolution shows the importance of precise wording in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما الحسيات فإذا ثبت الاشتراك الخ قال الأستاذ المحقق : قد اشتبه الكلام واضطرب ها هنا وفي المقصد السادس من المرصد السادس في بيان الضروريات، وذلك أنه قال المصنف : ها هنا كما هو المشهور إن الوجدانيات لعدم الاشتراك فيها لا تقوم حجة على الغير، ثم حكم الشارح على غيرها بأنها العمدة في العلوم لكونها حجةً على الغير أما البديهيات فمطلقاً، وأما سائر الأقسام، فإذا ثبت الاشتراك في الأسباب، ولقائل أن يقول : فإذا ثبت الاشتراك في الوجدان لمَ لاتقوم حجة على الغير؟ فإن قلت : الاشتراك في الوجدانيات مما لا يعلم قلت كذا في غيره سيما الحدسيات واعلم أن هذا إنما يرد على ما حمل الشارح كلام المتن عليه، حيث فسر قوله لأنها غير مشتركة بقوله : أي غير معلومة الاشتراك يقيناً، ففهم منه أنه يجوز الاشتراك فيها، وكذا قوله : فإن ذلك الغير ربما لم يجد من باطنه ما وجدناه، وأما إذا حملنا على ظاهره وقلنا الوجداني ما يجده الإنسان من نفسه كجوعه وعطشه، وأما ما يدركه من مثل خوف ،وغضب فإما بالاستدلال بالآثار وإما من قبيل الوهميات فلا يرد والظاهر أن الحق هذا، ولهذا صرح الإمام والمصنف بأنها غير مشتركة ويقولون في التمثيل كعلمنا بخوفنا وغضبنا، ثم قال الشارح في ذلك المقصد : اعلم أن العمدة من هذه المبادي الأوليات ثم القضايا غيره
His saying: 'And as for the sensed things, when the sharing in their causes is established' etc. The verifying master said: The speech has become confused and disordered here and in the sixth object of the sixth observatory on the exposition of necessary things. That is because the author said here, as is well-known, that the inner experiences (wijdāniyyāt) due to the lack of sharing in them do not constitute a proof against another. Then the commentator ruled regarding the other divisions that they are the mainstays in the sciences because they are a proof against another. As for the self-evidents, absolutely; and as for the remaining divisions, when the sharing in the causes is established. And an objector may say: If sharing in the inner experiences is established, why do they not constitute a proof against another? If you say: The sharing in inner experiences is not known, I say: Likewise in other divisions, especially the intuitive inferences. Know that this only refutes what the commentator understood from the text, where he interpreted his statement 'because they are not shared' as 'i.e., not known to be shared with certainty', so it is understood that sharing in them is possible. Similarly his statement: 'for that other perhaps does not find in his inner self what we found'. But if we take it at face value and say: The inner experience (al-wijdānī) is what a person finds in himself like his hunger and thirst; and as for what he perceives like fear and anger, that is either by deducing from effects or from the class of estimations (wahmiyyāt)—then no objection arises. And it appears that the truth is this, and for this reason the Imām and the author explicitly stated that they are not shared, and they say in their example: like our knowledge of our fear and anger. Then the commentator said in that object: Know that the mainstays of these principles are the first principles, then the propositions ... etc.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer regarding the status of inner experiences (وجدانيات). The objector notes a tension: if the commentator says that other divisions (like sensed things) are proofs when their causes are shared, then why wouldn't inner experiences also be proofs if their sharing is established? The objector argues that the lack of knowledge of sharing applies equally to other areas, like intuitive inferences. The response clarifies that this objection only applies if we interpret the text's 'not shared' as 'not known to be shared' (the commentator's interpretation). But if we take it literally—that inner experiences are simply not shared in reality (e.g., one person's hunger is not another's)—then there is no inconsistency. The commentator supports the literal view, noting that the Imām and the author affirm that inner experiences are not shared, giving examples like fear and anger. Thus the correct view is that inner experiences are inherently private, not just unknown to be shared.

Key Terms
الوجدانيات
الاشتراك
حجة
الوهميات
المشهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'inner experiences are not shared' means they are not known to be shared, and this is the intended meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that the literal interpretation—that inner experiences are not actually shared in reality—is correct and resolves the logical objection, as affirmed by the Imām and the author.

Checkpoint
According to the resolution in the text, what is the correct interpretation of 'inner experiences are not shared'?
A) They are not known to be shared
B) They are not actually shared in reality
C) They are shared but not useful
D) They are shared only among certain people

Key Takeaway
Inner experiences are not shared in reality, making them unsuitable as proofs for others; the literal interpretation of 'not shared' resolves the logical objection and is affirmed by authoritative figures.

Necessary Sciences: Definition and Division
The author defines necessary sciences, identifies two types, and states condition for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of necessary sciences, their two categories (sense-perceptions and self-evidents), and the conditions under which each can serve as proof.

Core Idea
Necessary sciences are the foundation of all knowledge and come in two types: self-evident and sense-based.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of necessary sciences in Islamic epistemology. The author defines necessary sciences as those that are primary or akin to primary, and then divides them into two categories: self-evident truths and sense-perceptions. Understanding this division is essential for grasping how knowledge is built and how arguments are validated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning. Note the structure: definition, classification, conditions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage states that necessary sciences are divided into two sections: sense-perceptions (حسيات) and self-evidents (بديهيات). Locate these terms and understand their roles.
Self-evidents are absolute proofs, while sense-perceptions require shared causes.
3
Understand the condition for sense-perceptions
The author explains that sense-perceptions become proof for others only if the commonality in their causes is established. These causes include experience, recurrent report, intuition, and observation.
This condition ensures that sense-perceptions are not private.
4
Examine the discussion on intuitions
The author addresses a concern about intuitions (حدسيات). He clarifies that intuitions often involve hidden syllogism and rely on sense perception for common people. Distinguish between intuitions with and without sense involvement.
Intuitions without sense are rare and belong to holy souls.
5
Review the explanation of 'primary ones'
The passage ends by explaining that 'self-evident' means necessary and includes all such cases. Innate syllogistic propositions are considered primary because the middle term does not separate from the extremes, so only the extremes need to be conceived.
This shows how some syllogisms are reduced to self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية أي الأوليات وما في حكمها من القضايا الفطرية القياس فهذان القسمان أعني الحسيات والبديهيات هما العمدة في العلوم وهما يقومان حجة على الغير، أما البديهيات فعلى الإطلاق وأما الحسيات فإذا ثبت الاشتراك في أسبابها أعني فيما يقتضيها من تجربة أو تواتر أو حدس أو مشاهدة ) بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي الحاصل بلا تجشم كسب إلا أنه لما كان التعويل فيها على ذلك القياس علماً يحصل للنفس بمجرد القياس الحدسي من غير استعانة بالحس منها، كمالاً لصاحب النفس القدسية، وإما بناء على أن المراد بما للحس مدخل فيها أعم من مدخليته في جميع أنواعها أو بعضها . قوله : ( أو مشاهدة ) أي إدراك بإحدى الحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة، ليعم الوهميات وهذا هو الحق، فإن مشاهداتك ليست حجة على ما لم يكن له ذلك المشعر والشعور، وإنما ترك هذا من النظريات إنما تحصل من مقدمات لا دخل للحس فيها وبالجملة من له قوة قدسية يحصل له العلوم بالحدس مع أنه لا دخل للحس في بعض العلوم قطعاً، والجواب أولاً أن الكلام في الضروريات العامة ولا حدس للعامة إلا ويتقدم الحس عليه مثل الحكم بأن نور القمر مستفاد الشمس، وثانياً أن ما أدخلناه في الحسيات من الحدسيات هو الذي للحس مدخل فيه، والبعض الآخر مندرج في البديهيات لأنه في حكم الأوليات كالقضايا الفطرية القياس في أن العقل لا يحتاج إلى نظر فتأمل . قوله : (أي الأوليات) وجه التفسير أن البديهي بمعنى الضروري يعم الكل، وكون فطرية القياس في حكم الأوليات بناء على أن الوسط لما لم يفارق تصور الطرفين فكأنه لا احتياج هناك للعقل إلا إلى تصور الطرفين.
The fourth observatory: on establishing the necessary sciences, i.e., the primary ones and what is in their ruling from the innate propositions of syllogism. So these two sections, namely the sense-perceptions and the self-evidents, are the foundation in the sciences and they stand as proof for others. As for the self-evidents, they are absolute; and as for the sense-perceptions, if the commonality in their causes is established, i.e., in what necessitates them from experience, recurrent report, intuition, or observation. [Then a discussion on intuitions: they require repetition of observation and comparison of hidden syllogism obtained without effort, except that reliance on that syllogism is knowledge obtained by the soul by mere intuitive syllogism without assistance from sense, as perfection for the owner of the holy soul, or based on the meaning of 'what has sense's involvement' being more general than its involvement in all its types or some.] He said: (or observation) i.e., perception by one of the external or internal senses, to include the estimations; and this is the truth. For your observations are not proof for one who did not have that perception and awareness. And this [discussion] is omitted from theoretical matters, for they are obtained from premises with no sense involvement. In summary, whoever has a holy power obtains sciences by intuition, even though sense has no involvement in some sciences definitively. The answer: first, the discussion is about general necessities, and there is no intuition for the common people except that sense precedes it, like the judgment that the light of the moon is derived from the sun. Second, what we have included in sense-perceptions from intuitions is that in which sense has involvement; and the other part is included in self-evidents because it is in the ruling of the primary ones, like the innate propositions of syllogism, in that the intellect does not need reflection. So reflect. His saying: (i.e., the primary ones) the explanation is that the self-evident in the sense of necessary encompasses all. And the innate-ness of syllogism being in the ruling of primary ones is based on the fact that the middle term, since it does not depart from the conception of the two extremes, it is as if there is no need for the intellect except to conceive the two extremes.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins by stating that the fourth observatory is about establishing necessary sciences. He then explains that 'necessary sciences' refer to the primary ones and what is akin to them, namely the innate propositions of syllogism. He identifies two main categories: sense-perceptions (حسيات) and self-evidents (بديهيات), which together form the foundation of all sciences and serve as proof for others. Self-evidents are absolutely proof, while sense-perceptions require the establishment of shared causes (experience, recurrent report, intuition, or observation) to be proof for others. The commentator then addresses a potential objection concerning intuitions (حدسيات), clarifying that they involve hidden syllogism and often rely on sense. He argues that general necessary sciences are intended, and common people's intuitions are always preceded by sense. The second part explains the phrase 'or observation' to include both external and internal senses, and emphasizes that one person's observation is not proof for another without shared awareness. Finally, the commentator explains 'i.e., the primary ones' by stating that 'self-evident' is synonymous with 'necessary' and includes all such cases, and that innate syllogistic propositions are considered primary because the middle term does not separate from the two extremes, so the intellect only needs to conceive the extremes.

Misconception: All knowledge gained by intuition is independent of sense perception.
Correction: The author argues that common intuitions are always preceded by sense perception, and only certain intuitions (for holy souls) may be independent, but general necessary sciences are based on sense.

Necessary sciences are divided into self-evidents and sense-perceptions, and sense-perceptions require shared causes to serve as proof for others.

The Four Human Stances on Necessary Sciences
Enumeration of four groups regarding necessary sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical stances people can take regarding the two types of necessary sciences (sense-perceptions and self-evidents) and the correct interpretation of the second group's criticism of sense-perceptions.

Core Idea
Four stances on necessary sciences reveal how reason relates to sensory and axiomatic knowledge.

The author now examines how different thinkers have related to the foundational sources of knowledge: sense-perception and self-evident truths. He identifies four logical possibilities, but only three are historically attested. This lesson unpacks the four groups and clarifies a common misinterpretation of the second group's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's classification come through.
Notice that only two groups are explicitly named.
2
Identify the four groups
Using the author's later statement about 'acceptance together, rejection together, and acceptance of one without the other', deduce the four logical groups: both accepted, both rejected, sense-only, self-evident-only. Recognize that only the first two are historically found.
3
Understand the clarification
The second group criticizes sense-perceptions. The author explains that this does not deny sensation entirely; rather, certainty requires additional factors beyond mere sense.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والناس فيهما فرق أربع حسب ( الفرقة الأولى المعترفون بهما وهم الأكثرون ( الظاهرون على الحق القويم والصراط المستقيم إلى العقائد الدينية وسائر المطالب اليقينية ( الفرقة الثانية القادحون في الحسيات فقط ( أي دون البديهيات (وهذا القدح ( ينسب إلى أفلاطون وأرسطو وبطلميوس وجالينوس) صرح بهذه النسبة الإمام الرازي ولما كان هذا القدح منهم مستبعداً جداً، أشار المصنف إلى تأويله على تقدير صحة النسبة إليهم بقوله: ( ولعلهم أرادوا ) بقولهم أن الحسيات غير يقينية ( أي جزم العقل) بالحسيات (ليس بمجرد الحس بل لا بد له (مع) الإحساس من ( أمور تنضم إليه) أي إلى الحس . قوله : ( باعتبار قبولهما معاً وردهما معاً وقبول إحداهما دون الأخرى ( وأما الاحتمالات العقلية باعتبار قبول بعض أحدهما الآخر، أو بعضه فبعيد عن الاعتبار لم يذهب إليه أحد.
And people regarding them are four groups: The first group: those who acknowledge both, and they are the majority, those who are apparent upon the right truth and the straight path to religious beliefs and all certain pursuits. The second group: those who criticize sense-perceptions only, i.e., without self-evidents; and this criticism is attributed to Plato, Aristotle, Ptolemy, and Galen—Imam al-Razi explicitly stated this attribution. And since this criticism from them is very improbable, the author indicated its interpretation upon the assumption of the correctness of the attribution to them by his saying: 'Perhaps they meant' by their saying that sense-perceptions are not certain, i.e., the mind's certainty regarding sense-perceptions is not by mere sense, but rather it requires, along with sensation, matters that are joined to it, i.e., to sense. He said: (Considering their acceptance together, rejection together, and acceptance of one without the other) and as for the mental possibilities considering acceptance of part of one with the whole of the other, or part of it, it is far from consideration, and no one has adopted it.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a classification and an objection-and-answer. The author first states that people are divided into four groups regarding the acceptance of the two types of necessary sciences (sense-perceptions and self-evidents). He then names the first group: those who accept both, who are the majority and follow the right path. The second group: those who reject only sense-perceptions, accepting self-evidents. This criticism is attributed to certain ancient philosophers. The author then explains that if this attribution is correct, their intention was not to deny sense-perceptions entirely, but to say that certainty does not come from mere sensation; rather, it requires additional factors. Finally, the commentator explains the phrase 'considering their acceptance together, rejection together, and acceptance of one without the other'—these are the four logical possibilities: both accepted, both rejected, accept sense-perceptions only, or accept self-evidents only. Any other combination (e.g., accepting part of one with the whole of the other) is not considered.

Misconception: The second group's rejection of sense-perceptions is an outright denial of sensory knowledge.
Correction: The author clarifies that they meant only that sensation alone does not produce certainty; additional conditions (e.g., intellect, external factors) are required.

Why Sense-Perceptions Are Not Sufficient for Certainty
The author rebuts the claim that sense-perceptions are not certain and explains the correct interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense-perceptions alone cannot guarantee certainty and how the rebuttal clarifies the intended argument.

Core Idea
Sense-perceptions are not certain by themselves; errors show they need additional verification.

This lesson examines an objection raised against the claim that sense-perceptions provide certainty. The author argues that if mere sensation sufficed, errors would not occur. Since errors do occur, sensation alone is insufficient. We will also see a subtle point about interpreting the opponents' argument correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is stated and followed by a commentator's remark.
The passage is short but packed: an objection and a meta-commentary.
2
Understand the core argument
Grasp the logical point: if sense-perceptions were certain through mere sensation, then error would be impossible. But errors do occur, so sense alone is insufficient for certainty.
This is a classic argument against naive empiricism.
3
Consider the nuance in the commentary
The commentator notes that the opponents' argument, as it appears, does not align with the interpretation that they only meant sensation is insufficient. This invites deeper reflection on what exactly the opponents intended.
This shows that theological debates often examine both the form and the intended meaning of arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ليس بمجرد الحس وإلا لما وقع الغلط فيها . قيل: احتجاجهم المذكور بظاهره لا يلائم حمل مدعاهم على هذه الإرادة .
His saying: (Not by mere sense, otherwise error would not occur in it). It was said: Their aforementioned argument in its outward form is not consistent with interpreting their claim according to this intention.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author quotes the text: 'Not by mere sense, otherwise error would not occur in it,' meaning that if sense-perceptions were certain solely by sensation, no errors would ever arise in sensory judgments. But errors do occur, so sense alone is insufficient. The commentator then notes a potential inconsistency: the outward form of the opponents' argument does not align with the interpretation that they merely meant that sensation is not sufficient for certainty. This suggests a nuanced debate about whether the criticism of sense-perceptions targets sensation itself or the certainty derived from it.

Key Terms
الحس
الغلط
الاحتجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the rebuttal completely denies the value of sense-perceptions, implying they are always false.
Correction: The rebuttal only shows that sense-perceptions are not sufficient for certainty; it does not deny that they can be reliable when combined with rational scrutiny.

Checkpoint

A) Because sense-perceptions are always false.
B) Because errors occur in sensory judgments.
C) Because self-evidents are more important.
D) Because the senses are not shared among people.

Key Takeaway
Sense-perceptions, while valuable, are not self-certifying; errors show they require rational scrutiny and are not sufficient for certainty on their own.

Why Sensory Judgment Cannot Give Universal Knowledge
Refutes sensory judgment for universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory perception cannot produce universal judgments that apply to all actual and potential individuals of a kind.

Core Idea
Sense only grasps present particulars, not universals spanning all times and possibilities.

Can our senses give us universal knowledge, like knowing that fire is always hot? This passage argues they cannot. It explains that sensory perception is limited to particular individuals at the moment of observation, and this limitation makes it impossible for sensory judgments to cover all actual, past, future, or even imaginary instances. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
The passage presents an objection that if sensory judgment were reliable, it would have to be about universals or particulars. Read the Arabic and translation to see how the author refutes the universal case.
Notice the author only fully refutes the universal case here; the particular case is mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Identify the core limitation of sense
The key claim is that sense only perceives individual objects like 'this fire' or 'that fire', not all fires. Ask yourself: can you ever perceive all fires of the past, present, and future through your senses? The answer is no.
3
Understand the scope of universal statements
The passage highlights that a universal statement like 'fire is hot' applies not only to existing fires but also to imaginary ones that could exist. This goes beyond what any sense can access.
This is a deeper point: universals cover even hypothetical individuals.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that sensory judgment cannot produce a universal judgment at all—neither a real universal (covering all actual individuals) nor an external one (covering all possible individuals). This is why sense cannot be the basis for universal propositions in theology or philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : لو اعتبر حكم الحس فإما في الكليات ) أي في القضايا الكلية ( أو في الجزئيات ( أي في الأحكام الجارية على الجزئيات الحقيقية، وكلاهما باطل أما الأول) وهو بطلان اعتبار حكمه في الكليات ( فظاهر) لأن الحس لا يدرك إلا هذه النار، وتلك النار لا جميع النيران الموجودة في الحال ولو فرض إدراكه إياها بأسرها فليس له تعلق قطعاً بأفرادها الماضية والمستقبلة، فلا يعطي حكماً كلياً على جميع أفرادها ( سيما وقد ذهب المحققون إلى أن الحكم في قولنا النار حارة ليس على كل نار موجودة في الخارج في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة فقط بل عليها وعلى جميع (الأفراد المتوهمة الوجود في الخارج أيضاً ولا شك أنه لا تعلق للحس بها ) أي بالأفراد المتوهمة ( البتة ) فكيف يعطي حكماً متناولاً إياها؟ والحاصل أن الحكم لا يعطي حكماً كلياً أصلاً لا حقيقياً ولا خارجياً، فلا يتصور اعتبار حكمه في الكليات قطعاً
They said: If the judgment of sense is considered, then either in universals (i.e., in universal propositions) or in particulars (i.e., in judgments applying to real particulars), and both are invalid. As for the first (i.e., the invalidity of considering its judgment in universals), it is apparent, because sense perceives only this fire, and that fire is not all fires existing at present; even if we suppose it perceives all of them, it has absolutely no connection with their past and future individuals, so it does not give a universal judgment over all its individuals. Especially since the investigators have held that the judgment in our statement 'fire is hot' is not only about every fire existing externally in one of the three times, but also about all imaginary individuals that could exist externally, and there is no doubt that sense has no connection with them at all, so how can it give a judgment encompassing them? The conclusion is that the judgment (of sense) does not give a universal judgment at all, neither real nor external, so it is absolutely inconceivable to consider its judgment in universals.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the skeptics (or rather, an objection that the author refutes) that if sensory judgment is to be considered reliable, it must be either in universals (kulliyat) or in particulars (juz'iyat). The author argues that both are invalid. For universals, the reason is that sense perception is limited to particular individuals present at the moment of perception. It does not perceive all individuals of a kind across all times (past, present, future) nor imaginary individuals. For example, when we see a fire, we only perceive that particular fire, not all fires. Even if we could perceive all existing fires at once, we still would not perceive past or future fires. Moreover, a universal statement like 'fire is hot' applies not only to actual fires but also to hypothetical fires that could exist, which sense cannot perceive at all. Therefore, sensory judgment cannot produce a genuine universal judgment (either real or external), so it is impossible to rely on sense for universals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a general impression from many observations (e.g., seeing many fires and noticing they are hot) suffices to form a universal judgment that all fires are hot.
Correction: The passage argues that even if one observed all existing fires, sense has no access to past, future, or hypothetical fires. A universal judgment requires covering all possible individuals, which sense cannot achieve.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that sensory judgment cannot apply to universals?
A) Sense always errs about universals.
B) Sense only perceives present individuals, not all past, future, or imaginary ones.
C) Universals are not real objects of perception.
D) The criterion for universals is logical, not empirical.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception is confined to particular individuals at a given moment; therefore, it cannot serve as the basis for universal judgments that claim to cover all real and possible instances.

Why Sensory Judgment Cannot Apply to Particulars
Sanusi refutes sensory judgment in particulars with examples of optical illusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory judgments about particular objects are considered unreliable in kalam methodology, due to their frequent errors as exemplified by optical illusions.

Core Idea
Our senses can deceive us, so they cannot be trusted for certain knowledge about particular objects.

Sanusi continues his discussion of judgment categories by addressing sensory judgment in particulars. He argues that sensory judgments are often mistaken, using visual illusions as evidence. This lesson examines his reasoning and the examples he gives to show why such judgments are not reliable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a main claim followed by supporting examples.
The passage is part of a larger discussion on the validity of different types of judgment.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the overall point Sanusi is making about sensory judgment in particulars? Find the sentence that states the conclusion.
Look for the phrase 'فلا يكون مقبولاً معتبراً'.
3
Examine the first example: distant fire
Read the explanation of why a distant fire appears larger. Understand the optical mechanism described (visual ray, illuminated air).
This example shows that sensory judgment can be mistaken in size perception.
4
Examine the second example: grape in water
Read the brief example of a grape appearing larger in water. Consider why this illustrates that sensory error is common.
The explanation ties into the extramission theory of vision, but focus on the fact of error.
5
Reflect on the implications
Why does frequent error make sensory judgment unreliable for particulars? How does this affect theological reasoning?
This connects to the broader theme of establishing sound methods for knowing Allah and the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني ) وهو بطلان اعتبار حكمه في الجزئيات ( فلان حكم الحس في الجزئيات يغلط كثيرا، وإذا كان كذلك فحكمه في أي جزئي كان في معرض الغلط، فلا يكون مقبولاً معتبراً وإنما قلنا يغلط كثيراً ( لوجوه الأول إنا نرى الصغير كبيراً كالنار البعيدة في الظلمة ) هذا إذا لم تكن بعيدة جداً، والسبب فيه أن ما حولها من الهواء يستضيء بضوئها والشعاع البصري المحاذي لما حولها لا ينفذ في الظلمة نفوذاً تاماً، فلا يتميز عند الرائي جرم النار عن الهواء المضيء بها المشابه بضوئه إياها، فيدركهما معاً جملة واحدة ويحسبهما ناراً، وإذا كانت قريبة نفذ الشعاع وامتازت النار عن الهواء المضيء بمجاورتها فأدركها على ما هي عليه من الصغر، وإذا كانت بعيدة جداً كانت كالمرئيات البعيدة التي ستعرف حالها ( وكالعنبة في الماء ترى كالإجاصة ) وسببه أن رؤية الأشياء على القول الأظهر إنما هي بخروج الشعاع على هيئة مخروط
And as for the second (i.e., the invalidity of considering its judgment in particulars), it is because the judgment of sense in particulars errs often, and if it is so, then its judgment concerning any particular is liable to error, so it is not acceptable or reliable. And we only say it errs often for several reasons: First, we see the small as large, like a distant fire in darkness — this if it is not very far. The reason for this is that the air around it is illuminated by its light, and the visual ray facing what is around it does not penetrate the darkness completely, so the body of the fire is not distinguished from the illuminated air resembling it in light, so one perceives both together as one whole and mistakes them for fire. And when it is near, the ray penetrates and the fire is distinguished from the illuminated air adjacent to it, so one perceives it as it actually is, as small. And if it is very distant, it becomes like distant visible objects whose state will be known. And like the grape in water appears like a plum. The reason for this is that, according to the more evident opinion, the vision of objects is by the emission of a ray in the form of a cone.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage completes the refutation by addressing the second possibility: sensory judgment in particulars. The author argues that sensory judgments about particulars are often erroneous, so any particular judgment is subject to error and thus not reliable. To demonstrate this, he gives two examples of visual illusions. First, a distant fire in darkness appears larger than it actually is. He explains the optical mechanism: the air around the fire is illuminated, and the visual ray cannot fully penetrate the darkness, so the observer confuses the fire's body with the surrounding illuminated air, perceiving a larger object. When the fire is close, the ray penetrates properly and the true size is discerned. If it is very far, it resembles other distant objects. Second, a grape submerged in water appears larger (like a plum). The explanation is tied to the theory of vision (extramission theory) where vision occurs via a cone of rays emitted from the eye. These examples show that sense perception can be deceived, so it cannot be trusted for certain knowledge about particulars.

الحس
الغلط
النار البعيدة
العنبة
الإجاصة
الشعاع البصري
المخروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that optical illusions are rare exceptions and do not affect the general reliability of the senses.
Correction: Sanusi argues that sensory error is frequent enough to render any particular sensory judgment unreliable, as shown by common illusions like the distant fire and grape in water.

Checkpoint
Which example does the author provide to illustrate sensory error in particulars?
A) A distant fire appears smaller.
B) A grape in water appears larger than it is.
C) A straight stick appears bent in water.
D) The moon appears larger at the horizon.

Key Takeaway
Sensory judgment about particular objects is unreliable because it frequently errs, as shown by visual illusions such as a distant fire appearing larger and a grape in water appearing larger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when your eyes deceived you—perhaps you mistook one thing for another. How did that moment affect your certainty about what you saw? After learning that sensory error is frequent, are you ready to distrust your own senses when they contradict rational evidence? Will you pause and verify before making a judgment about what is real?

The passage says that any particular sensory judgment is 'liable to error.' How does this challenge your daily reliance on your sight to confirm truth? When you look at the world around you, do you assume you see things as they are? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that your eyes may be tricking you? Will you seek deeper knowledge beyond appearances?
?
Consider the fire illusion: the fire seems large because the air around it is illuminated. In your own life, what 'illuminated air'—distractions, desires, or assumptions—might be causing you to perceive something as bigger or more important than it truly is? How can you use the lesson of sensory fallibility to strip away illusions and see reality as it is?

Sensory Judgment Fails: The Example of the Distant Fire
Sanusi demonstrates frequent sensory error with the fire example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory judgments are considered unreliable in particulars, using the example of a distant fire appearing larger than it is.

Core Idea
Sensory error is not rare—it is woven into the mechanism of perception.

This lesson introduces the core critique of sensory evidence in rational theology. The author begins by stating that sensory judgments in particular cases frequently err, so they cannot be accepted as certain. He then provides a concrete example: a distant fire in darkness appears much larger due to the illumination of surrounding air. Understanding this example establishes why sensory evidence is treated with caution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and note the opening statement: sensory judgments in particulars err frequently, so they are not acceptable.
The passage uses strong language: 'لا يكون مقبولاً معتبراً'.
2
Trace the fire example
Follow the explanation of why a distant fire in darkness appears large. The visual ray fails to penetrate the illuminated air, so the observer conflates fire and glowing air.
When the fire is near, the ray penetrates and the true size is seen.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how this everyday example shows that sensory error is not an exception but a frequent occurrence, undermining trust in sensory judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني ) وهو بطلان اعتبار حكمه في الجزئيات ( فلان حكم الحس في الجزئيات يغلط كثيرا، وإذا كان كذلك فحكمه في أي جزئي كان في معرض الغلط، فلا يكون مقبولاً معتبراً وإنما قلنا يغلط كثيراً ( لوجوه الأول إنا نرى الصغير كبيراً كالنار البعيدة في الظلمة ) هذا إذا لم تكن بعيدة جداً، والسبب فيه أن ما حولها من الهواء يستضيء بضوئها والشعاع البصري المحاذي لما حولها لا ينفذ في الظلمة نفوذاً تاماً، فلا يتميز عند الرائي جرم النار عن الهواء المضيء بها المشابه بضوئه إياها، فيدركهما معاً جملة واحدة ويحسبهما ناراً، وإذا كانت قريبة نفذ الشعاع وامتازت النار عن الهواء المضيء بمجاورتها فأدركها على ما هي عليه من الصغر، وإذا كانت بعيدة جداً كانت كالمرئيات البعيدة التي ستعرف حالها ( وكالعنبة في الماء ترى كالإجاصة ) وسببه أن رؤية الأشياء على القول الأظهر إنما هي بخروج الشعاع على هيئة مخروط مستدير رأسه عند الحدقة وقاعدته على سطح المرئي ويتفاوت مقدار صغراً وكبراً بحسب صغر زاوية رأس المخروط وكبرها، ثم إن الخطوط الشعاعية سطح المخروط الشعاعي تنفذ إلى المرئي على الاستقامة إلى طرفيه، إذا كان الشفاف المتوسط بين الرائي والمرئي متشابه الغلظ والرقة فإن فرض فيه تفاوت، بأن يكون مثلاً ما يلي الرائي رقيقاً كالهواء وما يلي المرئي غليظاً كالماء في مثالنا قوله : ( على القول الأظهر أي الأشهر بين الحكماء احتراز عن مذهب طائفة منهم، وهو أن الشيء المشف الذي بين البصر والمرئي يتكيف بكيفية الشعاع البصري، ويصير ذلك آلة للإبصار وعما ذهب إليه الإمام من أنه إذا قابل المرئي الرائي على وجه مخصوص، خلق الله الرؤية من عير اتصال شعاع ولا انطباع صورة . قوله : ( بخروج الشعاع ) المتحقق أو المتوهم فإنهم متفقون على أن الرؤية بخروج الشعاع على هيئة المخروط المستدبر، إلا أن الرياضيين يقولون : بأن الخروج متحقق والرؤية باتصال الشعاع بالمرئي من غير انطباع الصورة في الحدقة والطبيعيين يقولون : بأن الخروج متوهم والرؤية بالانطباع وتفصيله فيما سيأتي في بحث الإدراك بالبصر. قوله بحسب صغر زاوية رأس المخروط سواء كانت الزاوية متحققة أو متوهمة، والصورة منطبعة عندها أولاً قيل : كونه على هيئة المخروط المخصوص من الأصول الموضوعة للمناظر، وقد برهن عليه بعضهم وجعله من مسائل الفن وفيه تأمل . قوله : ( على هيئة مخروط) المخروط شكل مجسم تحيط به دائرة هي قاعدته، وسطح مستدير يقع منها على التضايف إلى نقطة رأسه فإن قلت : إذا كان المرئي مستديراً فظاهر أن المخروط مستدير وأما إذا كان مضلعاً فينبغي أن يكون المخروط أيضاً مضلعاً بناء على أن قائمته تنطبق على سطح المرئي قلت : لا يجب أن تنطبق قاعدته على سطح المرئي بل هي تشتمل على سطح المرئي وعلى أمور أخر غيره، نعم المخروط الصغير الداخل في المخروط الأول الحاصل من الخطوط الواصلة إلى سطح المرئي يكون تابعاً لسطح المرئي إن كان مضلعاً فمضلع، وإن كان مستديرا فمستدير قوله : (بحسب صغر زاوية رأس المخروط كلامه يدل صريحاً على حدوث زاوية الرؤية عند الحدقة وهو المشهور لكن لا يكفي في الإبصار الانطباع في الجليدية، وإلا يرى شيء واحد شيئين لانطباع صورته في جليدتي العينين بل لا بد من تأدي الصورة إلى العصبتين المجوفتين وإلى الحس المشترك، لا بمعنى انتقالها إليها إذا لا يجوز انتقال العرض بل بمعنى أن انطباعها الجليدية معد لفيضان الصورة على الملتقى وفيضانها عليه معد لفيضانها على الحس المشترك كما أن مقابلة المبصر للباصرة توجب استعدادا يفيض به صورته على الجليدية ولوجوب تأدي الصورة إلى الملتقى قال في بيان سبب رؤية الواحد كثيراً: وقعت الخطوط الشعاعية على المرئي من محاذاة واحدة هي ملتقاهما لا لأن زاوية الرؤية تحدث عند الملتقى كما ظن هذا بقي هاهنا بحث هو أن قوله : رؤية الأشياء على القول الخ يشعر بأن سياق كلامه على مذهب الرياضيين، وحديث الزاوية إنما يناسب مذهب الطبيعيين أعني القول بالانطباع أو
As for the second (point), which is the invalidity of considering its judgment regarding particulars, it is because the judgment of sense concerning particulars errs frequently. And if so, then its judgment concerning any particular is prone to error, so it is not acceptable or considered. And we only said it errs frequently (for reasons: First: we see the small as large, like a distant fire in darkness) this is when it is not very distant. The reason is that the air around it is illuminated by its light, and the visual ray aligned with what is around it does not penetrate the darkness completely, so the fire's body is not distinguished by the observer from the illuminated air resembling it in light, so he perceives both together as one whole and considers them fire. And when it is near, the ray penetrates and the fire is distinguished from the adjacent illuminated air, so he perceives it as it is in its smallness. And when it is very distant, it is like distant visible objects whose case will be known. (And like a grape in water appears like a pear). And its cause is that the vision of things, according to the most apparent opinion, is only by the emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone whose vertex is at the pupil and its base on the surface of the visible object, and its size varies small and large according to the smallness and largeness of the angle of the cone's vertex. Then the radial lines on the surface of the cone penetrate the visible object straight to its ends, if the transparent medium between the observer and the visible object is uniform in thickness and thinness. If variation is supposed, such that what is near the observer is thin like air and what is near the visible object is thick like water in our example. His statement: (according to the most apparent opinion) i.e., the most famous among the philosophers, cautioning against the view of a group of them, which is that the transparent thing between the eye and the visible object is conditioned by the quality of the visual ray, and it becomes an instrument for vision, and contrary to what al-Imam held that when the visible object faces the observer in a specific manner, God creates vision without connection of a ray nor impression of an image. His statement: (by emission of the ray) actual or imagined, for they agree that vision is by emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone, except that the mathematicians say that the emission is actual and vision is by the connection of the ray to the visible object without impression of the image in the pupil, while the natural philosophers say that the emission is imagined and vision is by impression, and its detail will come in the discussion of perception by sight. His statement: depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex, whether the angle is actual or imagined, and the image is impressed at it or not. It is said: its being in the form of a specific cone is one of the principles laid down for optics, and some have demonstrated it and made it among the matters of the art, but there is reflection in it. His statement: (in the form of a cone) the cone is a solid figure surrounded by a circle which is its base, and a curved surface that tapers from it to its vertex. If you say: if the visible object is circular, then it is apparent that the cone is circular, but if it is polygonal, then the cone should also be polygonal based on its lateral surface conforming to the surface of the visible object. I say: its base does not have to conform to the surface of the visible object; rather it includes the surface of the visible object and other things besides it. Yes, the small inner cone within the first cone resulting from the lines reaching the surface of the visible object follows the surface of the visible object: if polygonal then polygonal, if circular then circular. His statement: (depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex) his words clearly indicate the occurrence of the visual angle at the pupil, which is well-known. However, impression in the crystalline lens is not sufficient for vision; otherwise one thing would be seen as two due to the impression of its image in the two crystalline lenses of the two eyes. Rather, it is necessary that the image be conveyed to the two hollow nerves and to the common sense, not meaning its transfer to them (since transfer of an accident is not permissible) but that its impression in the crystalline lens prepares for the emanation of the image upon the meeting point (المتلقي), and its emanation upon it prepares for its emanation upon the common sense, just as the facing of the visible object to the observer necessitates a preparedness for the emanation of its image upon the crystalline lens. And due to the necessity of conveying the image to the meeting point, he said, in explaining the cause of seeing one thing as many: the radial lines fall upon the visible object from one alignment which is their meeting point, not because the visual angle occurs at the meeting point as was thought. There remains here a discussion: that his statement 'vision of things according to the opinion...' suggests that his context follows the mathematicians' school, while the discussion of the angle is appropriate only to the naturalists' school, i.e., the opinion of impression or...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage refutes the reliability of sensory judgments even in particulars by showing they frequently err. It begins by stating that sensory judgments in particulars err often, so any particular judgment is suspect. Then it gives two faces (reasons): First, we see a small fire as large when it is distant in darkness, because the visual ray does not penetrate the illuminated air surrounding the fire, so the observer conflates the fire with the glowing air. When the fire is nearer, the ray penetrates and the true size is perceived. Second, a grape in water appears larger like a pear due to refraction when the medium is not uniform (water vs air). The explanation of these errors leads to a detailed discussion of the mechanism of vision. According to the dominant view (of philosophers), vision occurs by a ray emitted in a cone shape from the eye to the object. The size of the visual angle determines perceived size. The passage then discusses different schools: mathematicians hold that the ray actually emits and vision is by contact without image impression; natural philosophers hold that the ray is imagined and vision is by impression of the image in the eye. It also discusses the necessity of the image reaching the common sense via the optic nerves, and clarifies that a single object is seen as one due to the convergence of rays at the meeting point, not because the visual angle originates there. The overall argument is that sensory perception is unreliable due to physical and physiological factors, thus sensory judgments cannot be accepted as certain, whether universal or particular.

Key Terms
حكم الحس
جزئيات
غلط
شعاع بصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Optical illusions are rare exceptions to reliable senses.
Correction: This passage argues that sensory error in particulars is frequent, as demonstrated by common experiences like a distant fire appearing larger.

Checkpoint
Why does a distant fire in darkness appear larger than it actually is?
A) The fire emits more light at a distance.
B) The visual ray fails to penetrate the illuminated air, so the observer conflates fire and air.
C) The angle of vision is larger for distant objects.
D) The air around the fire magnifies the image.

Key Takeaway
Sensory judgments are unreliable because they frequently err due to the mechanics of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your eyes every day. Think of a time when you were absolutely certain of something you saw, only to discover you were mistaken. How did that affect your trust in your senses? What does this lesson demand you do the next time you are tempted to say 'I saw it with my own eyes' as a proof of something in your faith?
?
The passage demonstrates that sensory error is not rare but woven into perception. How often do you use what you see as the final authority in matters of your deen? What would change if you held all sensory evidence with the caution this lesson teaches?
?
If you cannot trust your eyes about something as simple as the size of a fire, what makes you think you can trust them about more complex matters like witnessing miracles or judging others? Are you ready to re-evaluate how much weight you give to your senses in your daily practice of Islam?

The Optics of Refraction: Grape in Water and the Cone Theory
Sanusi uses the grape illusion to launch into optical theory and school debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the cone theory of vision explains the grape-in-water illusion and why philosophers debate the mechanism of sight.

Core Idea
Vision is not simple—it involves cones, angles, and debates about rays and impressions.

Having established that sensory error is frequent, the author provides a second example: a grape in water appears larger like a pear. This leads into a detailed discussion of the theory of vision. The passage explains the cone emission model, the role of the visual angle, and the disagreement between mathematicians and natural philosophers on whether an actual ray or an impression causes sight. This lesson unpacks that dense optical discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the grape example and its cause
The grape in water appears larger due to refraction when the medium is not uniform (air vs water). Note that this illusion also shows sensory error.
The cause given leads into the cone theory.
2
Understand the cone theory of vision
According to the dominant view, vision occurs by a ray emitted in a cone shape from the eye. The perceived size depends on the visual angle at the vertex.
The cone's base is on the object, vertex at the pupil.
3
Distinguish the two schools
Mathematicians believe the ray actually emits and vision is by contact; natural philosophers believe the ray is imagined and vision is by image impression in the eye.
This disagreement shows that even the mechanism of vision is debated.
4
Reflect on the implication for sensory reliability
The complexity and uncertainty in the theory of vision further support the conclusion that sensory judgments are not reliable.

As for the second (point), which is the invalidity of considering its judgment regarding particulars, it is because the judgment of sense concerning particulars errs frequently. And if so, then its judgment concerning any particular is prone to error, so it is not acceptable or considered. And we only said it errs frequently (for reasons: First: we see the small as large, like a distant fire in darkness) this is when it is not very distant. The reason is that the air around it is illuminated by its light, and the visual ray aligned with what is around it does not penetrate the darkness completely, so the fire's body is not distinguished by the observer from the illuminated air resembling it in light, so he perceives both together as one whole and considers them fire. And when it is near, the ray penetrates and the fire is distinguished from the adjacent illuminated air, so he perceives it as it is in its smallness. And when it is very distant, it is like distant visible objects whose case will be known. (And like a grape in water appears like a pear). And its cause is that the vision of things, according to the most apparent opinion, is only by the emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone whose vertex is at the pupil and its base on the surface of the visible object, and its size varies small and large according to the smallness and largeness of the angle of the cone's vertex. Then the radial lines on the surface of the cone penetrate the visible object straight to its ends, if the transparent medium between the observer and the visible object is uniform in thickness and thinness. If variation is supposed, such that what is near the observer is thin like air and what is near the visible object is thick like water in our example. His statement: (according to the most apparent opinion) i.e., the most famous among the philosophers, cautioning against the view of a group of them, which is that the transparent thing between the eye and the visible object is conditioned by the quality of the visual ray, and it becomes an instrument for vision, and contrary to what al-Imam held that when the visible object faces the observer in a specific manner, God creates vision without connection of a ray nor impression of an image. His statement: (by emission of the ray) actual or imagined, for they agree that vision is by emission of the ray in the form of a circular cone, except that the mathematicians say that the emission is actual and vision is by the connection of the ray to the visible object without impression of the image in the pupil, while the natural philosophers say that the emission is imagined and vision is by impression, and its detail will come in the discussion of perception by sight. His statement: depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex, whether the angle is actual or imagined, and the image is impressed at it or not. It is said: its being in the form of a specific cone is one of the principles laid down for optics, and some have demonstrated it and made it among the matters of the art, but there is reflection in it. His statement: (in the form of a cone) the cone is a solid figure surrounded by a circle which is its base, and a curved surface that tapers from it to its vertex. If you say: if the visible object is circular, then it is apparent that the cone is circular, but if it is polygonal, then the cone should also be polygonal based on its lateral surface conforming to the surface of the visible object. I say: its base does not have to conform to the surface of the visible object; rather it includes the surface of the visible object and other things besides it. Yes, the small inner cone within the first cone resulting from the lines reaching the surface of the visible object follows the surface of the visible object: if polygonal then polygonal, if circular then circular. His statement: (depending on the smallness of the angle of the cone's vertex) his words clearly indicate the occurrence of the visual angle at the pupil, which is well-known. However, impression in the crystalline lens is not sufficient for vision; otherwise one thing would be seen as two due to the impression of its image in the two crystalline lenses of the two eyes. Rather, it is necessary that the image be conveyed to the two hollow nerves and to the common sense, not meaning its transfer to them (since transfer of an accident is not permissible) but that its impression in the crystalline lens prepares for the emanation of the image upon the meeting point (المتلقي), and its emanation upon it prepares for its emanation upon the common sense, just as the facing of the visible object to the observer necessitates a preparedness for the emanation of its image upon the crystalline lens. And due to the necessity of conveying the image to the meeting point, he said, in explaining the cause of seeing one thing as many: the radial lines fall upon the visible object from one alignment which is their meeting point, not because the visual angle occurs at the meeting point as was thought. There remains here a discussion: that his statement 'vision of things according to the opinion...' suggests that his context follows the mathematicians' school, while the discussion of the angle is appropriate only to the naturalists' school, i.e., the opinion of impression or...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage refutes the reliability of sensory judgments even in particulars by showing they frequently err. It begins by stating that sensory judgments in particulars err often, so any particular judgment is suspect. Then it gives two faces (reasons): First, we see a small fire as large when it is distant in darkness, because the visual ray does not penetrate the illuminated air surrounding the fire, so the observer conflates the fire with the glowing air. When the fire is nearer, the ray penetrates and the true size is perceived. Second, a grape in water appears larger like a pear due to refraction when the medium is not uniform (water vs air). The explanation of these errors leads to a detailed discussion of the mechanism of vision. According to the dominant view (of philosophers), vision occurs by a ray emitted in a cone shape from the eye to the object. The size of the visual angle determines perceived size. The passage then discusses different schools: mathematicians hold that the ray actually emits and vision is by contact without image impression; natural philosophers hold that the ray is imagined and vision is by impression of the image in the eye. It also discusses the necessity of the image reaching the common sense via the optic nerves, and clarifies that a single object is seen as one due to the convergence of rays at the meeting point, not because the visual angle originates there. The overall argument is that sensory perception is unreliable due to physical and physiological factors, thus sensory judgments cannot be accepted as certain, whether universal or particular.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers agree on how vision works.
Correction: This passage shows a major disagreement between mathematicians and natural philosophers, indicating that even experts disagree on the mechanism of sight.

?

?

Why Squinting Causes Double Vision: Two Theories
Explains the phenomenon of double vision when squinting and presents two theories of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anatomical reason for double vision when squinting and the competing theories of normal single vision from the mathematicians and physicists.

Core Idea
Double vision occurs when the two optic nerves misalign, splitting the visual image.

Have you ever squinted and seen two moons? This lesson examines that very phenomenon through medieval Islamic optics. The author first explains the anatomy of vision—how two hollow nerves meet before reaching the eyes—then presents two rival theories about why we normally see one object instead of two. Understanding this debate reveals the sophistication of pre-modern neuroscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the phenomenon
Read the Arabic passage once to become familiar with the phenomenon of double vision when squinting. Note that this is presented as a known fact.
The passage begins with 'you see the one as many, like the moon when squinting.'
2
Understand the anatomical explanation
The author describes the optic nerves (عصبتان مجوفتان) that meet before reaching the eyes. When straight, the visual lines align; when deviated, they misalign, causing double vision. Trace this reasoning.
3
Compare the two theories of normal vision
The mathematicians attribute single vision to simultaneous ray emission; the physicists to a single image at the meeting point. Contrast these explanations.
Both theories account for why one object is seen as one under normal conditions.
4
Reflect on the role of the common sense
The physicists mention that sight is completed by the image at the meeting point and in the common sense (الحس المشترك). Consider why this concept is important.
This distinguishes the physicists' view from a purely optical account.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصلاً ( و) ترى ( الواحد كثيراً كالقمر إذا نظرنا إليه مع غمز إحدى العينين)، وذلك لأن النور البصري يمتد من الدماغ في عصبتين مجوفتين تتلاقيان قبل وصولهما إلى العينين ثم تتباعدان وتتصل كل واحدة منهما بواحدة من العينين، فالعصبتان إذا كانتا مستقيمتين وقعت الخطوط الشعاعية على المرئي من محاذاة واحدة هي ملتقاهما، فيرى واحداً فإذا انحرفتا أو انحرفت إحداهما امتدت تلك الخطوط إلى المرئي من محاذاتين فيرى لذلك اثنين. قوله فيرى (واحداً لوقوع الشعاع الخارج من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة عند الرياضيين ولحصول صورة واحدة في الملتقى عند الطبيعيين والإبصار إنما يتم بحصول الصورة فيه بل وفي الحس المشترك لا بمجرد الانطباع في الجليدية وإلا لرئي الشيء الواحد شيئين، فيرى ذلك اثنين لعدم الشعاع من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة بل على التعاقب عند الرياضيين وبحصول الصورة في الموضعين من الملتقى لأجل المحاذاتين عند الطبيعيين.
Originally, you see the one as many, like the moon when we look at it with squinting of one eye. That is because the visual light extends from the brain in two hollow nerves that meet before reaching the eyes, then separate, and each connects to one eye. So if the two nerves are straight, the radial lines fall on the seen object from one alignment, which is their meeting point, so it is seen as one. But if they deviate or one of them deviates, those lines extend to the seen object from two alignments, so for that reason it is seen as two. His saying: 'so it is seen as one' due to the rays from both eyes falling on the seen object at once according to the mathematicians, and due to the formation of a single image at the meeting point according to the physicists. Sight is only completed by the formation of the image in it (the meeting point) and in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), not merely by the impression in the crystalline lens; otherwise one thing would be seen as two. So it is seen as two because the rays from the eyes do not fall on the seen object at once but successively according to the mathematicians, and because the image is formed in two places at the meeting point due to the two alignments according to the physicists.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of seeing one object as two when one squints (غمز إحدى العينين). The author first describes the anatomy: the optic nerves (عصبتان مجوفتان) originate from the brain, meet before reaching the eyes, then separate. When the nerves are straight, the visual lines from each eye converge on a single alignment (محاذاة واحدة), so the object is seen as one. If the nerves are deviated, the lines come from two alignments, causing double vision. Then the author presents two theories from the mathematicians (الرياضيون) and physicists (الطبيعيون) to explain why sight normally sees one. The mathematicians say the rays (شعاع بصري) from both eyes strike the object simultaneously, producing a single perception. The physicists say a single image is formed at the meeting point (الملتقى) of the nerves, and this image is conveyed to the common sense (الحس المشترك). If that were not the case, one thing would be seen as two. In the case of squinting, the rays do not strike simultaneously (mathematicians) or the image is formed at two points (physicists), resulting in double vision.

عصبتان مجوفتان
ملتقى
الحس المشترك
شعاع بصري
الرياضيون
الطبيعيون

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Double vision is caused by the eye itself, not by the misalignment of the two images at the meeting point.
Correction: The passage explains that double vision results from the deviation of the optic nerves, causing two separate images at the meeting point, not from a problem within the eye.

Checkpoint
According to the physicists, why do we normally see one object as one?
A) Because the rays from both eyes fall on the object simultaneously.
B) Because a single image is formed at the meeting point of the optic nerves.
C) Because the crystalline lens produces a single image.
D) Because the brain ignores the second image.

Key Takeaway
The phenomenon of double vision when squinting reveals the brain's role in merging two images into one, a process explained by two ancient theories.

?

?
If even your eyes can be tricked by a slight squint into seeing double, what does that imply about your certainty in other matters you consider self-evident? Will you commit to examining one assumption today that you have always taken for granted?

The Double Moon Illusion: Reflection of Light
Explains the second case of double moon illusion via reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the optical principle of reflection that causes the moon to appear double when near the horizon, and why this only happens at low altitudes.

Core Idea
The moon seems double because direct and reflected rays reach the eye simultaneously near the horizon.

Have you ever noticed the moon appearing as two when it rises over water? This phenomenon is not a split of the moon but a play of light. In this lesson, we explore the second of two cases: how a visual ray from the eye both goes directly to the moon and reflects off water, creating two images. We'll also see why this only works when the moon is low.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to get a sense of the explanation.
Notice that the passage refers back to a previous case.
2
Identify the two optical rays
Look for the terms 'penetrating ray' (الشعاع النافذ) and 'reflected ray' (الشعاع المنعكس). Understand their paths.
The penetrating ray goes directly to the sky-moon; the reflected ray bounces off water.
3
Understand the horizon condition
Ask yourself why the phenomenon is limited to when the moon is near the horizon. The passage explains it.
The two images are seen simultaneously only near the horizon.
4
Relate to the first case
The passage mentions 'the first case' from earlier—squinting. See how this second case is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوعه وكونه قريباً من الأفق فإنا نراه على التقديرين قمرين أما على التقدير الأول فلما مر وأما على الثاني فلأن الشعاع البصري ينفذ في الهواء إلى قمر السماء، وينعكس من سطح الماء إليه أيضاً فيرى مرة في السماء بالشعاع النافذ، ومرة في الماء بالشعاع المنعكس. قوله : ( وينعكس من سطح الماء وذلك لأن وضع قمر السماء عن سطح الماء كوضع قمر الماء، ولذا قالوا : لو رصد ارتفاع قمر السماء بالآلة وانحطاط قمر الماء بها في دائرة الارتفاع لوجد كل واحد منهما مساوياً للآخر، وإنما شرط كونه عند طلوعه مع أنه كذلك عند كونه مرتفعاً لأن ذينك القمرين إنما يريان دفعة واحدة حال كونهما قريبين من الأفق وأما عند تباعدهما فيرى أحدهما بعد الآخر بتقليب الحدقة والالتفات إليه.
Or when we look at the water at its rising, while it is near the horizon, we see it in both cases as two moons. In the first case, for the reason that passed; in the second, because the visual ray penetrates the air to the moon of the sky and is reflected from the surface of the water to it as well, so it is seen once in the sky by the penetrating ray and once in the water by the reflected ray. His saying: 'And it is reflected from the surface of the water' because the position of the moon of the sky relative to the water surface is like the position of the image of the moon in the water. Therefore they said: if one observed the altitude of the sky-moon with an instrument and the depression of the water-moon with it in the altitude circle, each would be found equal to the other. The condition that it be at its rising, even though it is also so when it is high, is because those two moons are seen at once only when they are near the horizon. When they are distant, one is seen after the other by turning the pupil and looking towards it.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains another example of seeing one object as two: the moon appearing as two moons when it is near the horizon and there is water. The author distinguishes two cases: first, the case already mentioned (squinting) gives the same effect; second, when looking at the moon near the horizon, the visual ray (الشعاع البصري) goes directly to the sky-moon (قمر السماء) and also reflects from the water surface to the eye, producing two images simultaneously. The commentator adds that the position of the sky-moon relative to the water is such that the reflected image appears equal in altitude. The moon is seen double only when it is near the horizon; when it is high, the two images are seen one after the other due to the necessity of turning the eye.

Key Terms
شعاع نافذ
شعاع منعكس
قمر السماء
قمر الماء
الأفق
ارتفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon actually splits into two when near the horizon.
Correction: It is only an optical illusion due to the reflection of light; no physical split occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the moon appear as two only when it is near the horizon and not when it is high in the sky?
A) Because the direct and reflected rays reach the eye at the same time only when the moon is near the horizon.
B) Because the water is only present at the horizon.
C) Because the eye is more sensitive near the horizon.
D) Because the moon is actually smaller near the horizon.

Key Takeaway
The double moon illusion is due to simultaneous perception of direct and reflected rays, which only occurs near the horizon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What does this say about the limits of your ability to perceive truth at once? Are there 'horizons' in your understanding of your own faith that you have not yet approached, causing you only to see partial truths?
?
If a simple physical phenomenon can fool everyone, how much more careful must you be when evaluating claims about unseen realities? What step will you take today to verify a belief you hold, rather than accepting it because it 'makes sense' at first glance?

Artificial and natural cross-eyedness: the role of habit
Distinguishes artificial from natural cross-eyedness and explains habit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between artificial and natural cross-eyedness and how habit influences visual perception.

Core Idea
Habit can correct visual errors, even in natural cross-eyedness.

This lesson examines cross-eyedness as an example of visual error. It distinguishes between intentionally induced cross-eyedness and natural cross-eyedness, and explains why the latter rarely causes double vision due to habitual adjustment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the contrast between artificial and natural cross-eyedness.
Pay attention to the term 'اعتياد' (habit).
2
Identify the two types
Locate where artificial cross-eyedness (الحول الجعلي) is described and where natural cross-eyedness (الحول الفطري) is described. Note the different outcomes.
3
Understand the role of habit
Focus on the explanation for natural cross-eyedness: why does he rarely see double? The passage says it is due to habituation (اعتياده) to aligning the rays.
Contrast this with the artificial case where deviation causes double vision.
4
Reflect on the commentary
Read the commentary section to see how the commentator expands on the idea of using the two senses in a certain position.
Consider why the commentator says 'rarely' (فقلما).

And like the cross-eyed (al-ahwal), i.e., one who intentionally makes himself cross-eyed, for he sees the one as two, due to the occurrence of deviation in the two nerves or in one of them. As for the naturally cross-eyed (al-ahwal al-fitri), he rarely sees the one as two, because of his habituation to arriving at the truth. And conversely, i.e., he sees the many as one. His saying: 'Because of his habituation to arriving at the truth' by using the two senses in the position in which the rays emanating from them fall from one alignment, so he sees one. His saying: 'i.e., one who intentionally makes himself cross-eyed' – it was said: then that is sufficient to dispense with the report about squinting in the moon, because that is a case of artificial cross-eyedness. But you know that the purpose is to multiply examples of error, so there is no harm in generalizing after mentioning one example, nor in applying it to other examples.

Commentary
This passage discusses cross-eyedness (الحول) as another example of visual error. It distinguishes artificial cross-eyedness (الحول الجعلي) – where one intentionally strains to cross his eyes – from natural cross-eyedness (الحول الفطري). The artificial cross-eyed person sees one as two because the nerves are deliberately deviated. The natural cross-eyed person rarely sees double because he has become habituated (اعتياد) to adjusting his vision to see correctly. The commentary adds that the natural cross-eyed person uses his two eyes in a way that makes the rays fall from one alignment, thus seeing one. If he tried to use his eyes like a normal person, he would see double. The commentator remarks that finally (فقلما) he rarely sees double. There is a remark that the example of intentional cross-eyedness could replace the squinting example, but the author intends to multiply examples.
