The Fourth Type of Sensory Error and the Four Causes
The author defines the fourth type of sensory error with the example of snow and lists the four causes of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth type of sensory error, the four causes of error in perception, and why the fourth cause is distinguished from the first.

Core Idea
Sensory errors can deceive us even when we trust our senses.

In this passage, the author examines a specific type of sensory error where we mistake reflected light for color, using snow as an example. He then provides a systematic classification of all causes of sensory error, distinguishing between errors known at the time and those only recognized after reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about every detail. Then read the translation to get a clear understanding of the main points.
The passage includes both the author's own words and commentary within square brackets.
2
Identify the four causes of error
The passage lists four causes: (1) something in the sense organ, (2) something in the sentient, (3) similarity, (4) resemblance. Find where each is mentioned and note the examples.
3
Examine the snow example
The fourth cause is illustrated with snow: it appears white but is actually composed of transparent particles. Understand why this is considered a sensory error.
4
Understand the distinction between first and fourth causes
The commentary explains that the first cause involves an error recognized immediately, while the fourth cause's error is only realized after reflection. This is why they are separate categories.
This distinction is crucial for the author's classification.

الوجه (الرابع) وهو الدال على غلط الحس في الجزئيات التي نظنها محسوسة، وليست بمحسوسة حقيقة أنا نرى الثلج في غاية البياض مع أنه ليس بأبيض (أصلا) فإنا إذا تأملنا علمنا أنه مركب من أجزاء شفافة لا لون لها وهي الأجزاء المائية الرشية
قوله: (وليست بمحسوسة (حقيقة بل المحسوس حقيقة ما يشبهه ووجه الضبط في الوجوه الأربعة، أن سبب الغلط، إما أمر في الحاسة كضيق الزاوية وسعتها والانحراف والاشتغال بشيء آخر وعدم تبدل الوضع وهو الوجه الأول وفي الحاس وهو الوجه الثالث، وفي المحسوس فأما التماثل، وهو الوجه الثاني، أو التشابه وهو الوجه الرابع، وأما إيراد سراب في الوجه الأول فقد عرفت حاله .
قوله : ( فإنا إذا تأملنا (الخ اندفع بهذا ما قيل : يجوز أن يكون سبب تخيله سبب حدوثه فلا نسلم أن البياض ليس بموجود.
خفي كما في بياض الثلج مثلا بخلاف البديهي العقلي نحو الأربعة زوج، فإن العقل لا يجد فيه احتمالاً للفردية ..
قوله : (الرابع) أنا نرى الثلج في غاية البياض فيه بحث لأنه من اشتباه الضوء المنعكس باللون، وكلاهما مبصران بالذات، فظاهره من قبيل السراب، وقد عد في الوجه الأول اللهم إلا أن يقال : فرق بين الوجه الرابع والوجه الأول بأن الأول دال على غلط يعرفه الغالط حال الغلط، بخلاف الرابع فإنه لا يعرف الغالط فيه غلطه إلا بعد التأمل والإمعان ولهذا لا يعرفه العوام، وبهذا الاعتبار أفرز الرابع عن الأول وأما قوله نظنها محسوسة وليست بمحسوسة، فباعتبار أن اللون ليس محسوساً فيما ذكر من الصور مع أن الغالط يظن أن فيه لوناً محسوساً، فلا محذور فيه أيضاً فتأمل .
The fourth aspect, which indicates the error of sense in the particulars that we suppose are sensed, and they are not truly sensed: we see snow in extreme whiteness while it is not white at all, for if we reflect we know that it is composed of transparent particles with no color, and they are the watery fine particles.
His statement: (and they are not truly sensed, rather what is truly sensed is what resembles it. And the manner of control in the four aspects is that the cause of error is either something in the sense organ, like narrowness of angle and its width, deviation, preoccupation with something else, and lack of change in position, which is the first aspect; or in the sentient, which is the third aspect; or in the perceived object: either similarity, which is the second aspect, or resemblance, which is the fourth aspect. As for the mention of a mirage in the first aspect, you already know its case.
His statement: (for if we reflect, etc.) This repels what was said: it is possible that the cause of its appearance is the cause of its occurrence, so we do not concede that whiteness does not exist. Hidden as in the whiteness of snow, for example, unlike the rational self-evident like 'four is even', for the intellect does not find in it any possibility of oddness.
His statement: (the fourth) we see snow in extreme whiteness—there is discussion in it, because it is a confusion of reflected light with color, and both are perceptible in themselves, so its appearance is of the sort of mirage, and it has been counted in the first aspect. But it might be said: a difference between the fourth aspect and the first is that the first indicates an error that the mistaken person knows at the time of error, unlike the fourth, for the mistaken person does not know his error in it except after reflection and scrutiny, and therefore common people do not know it. By this consideration the fourth was separated from the first. And as for his statement 'we suppose them sensed and they are not sensed', it is because the color is not sensed in the mentioned forms, while the mistaken person supposes that there is a sensed color in it, so there is no harm in it either. So reflect.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of sensory error and lists the four causes of error. The author states that the fourth aspect indicates error in the particulars we suppose are sensed but are not truly sensed. The example is snow which appears intensely white but is not white at all; it is composed of transparent particles with no color. Then the commentary explains the four causes of error: (1) something in the sense organ itself, like narrowness or width of angle, deviation, preoccupation, or lack of change in position; (2) something in the sentient (the person); (3) something in the perceived object, either similarity (tamathul) or resemblance (tashabuh). The example of a mirage is included in the first cause. The commentary further discusses the distinction between the first and fourth causes: in the first, the person is aware of the error at the time, whereas in the fourth, the error is only recognized after reflection, so it is a separate category. The passage also addresses an objection about the possibility that the cause of the perception of whiteness could be the cause of its occurrence, and refutes that by noting that the intellect finds no possibility of oddness in mental intuitions like 'four is even', unlike sensory illusions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth cause of sensory error is the same as the first cause.
Correction: The first cause involves error that the person recognizes at the time of error (e.g., mirage), whereas the fourth cause involves error that is only recognized after reflection (e.g., snow appearing white). Thus, they are distinct.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the fourth cause of sensory error from the first cause according to the commentary?
A) The fourth involves the sense organ, the first involves the perceived object
B) In the first, the error is known at the time; in the fourth, it is only known after reflection
C) The fourth is caused by similarity, the first by resemblance
D) The fourth is caused by light, the first by distance

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of sensory error involves resemblance, where the error is only recognized after reflection, distinguishing it from errors known at the time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you content to remain among those who accept surface appearances without scrutiny, or will you commit to the discipline of tafakkur (reflection) as a habit? ?

Three Examples of Sensory Error: Snow, Powdered Glass, and Glass Crack
The author explains the physical cause of whiteness and presents three examples to prove sensory error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses progressively stronger examples of perceived whiteness to prove that sensory perception can be mistaken, and how he refutes the claim that mixture (mizaj) is the cause of color.

Core Idea
Whiteness can be an illusion; sensory error is proven when no mixture exists.

The author of the Sharh al-Mawaqif continues his critique of the claim that colors are caused by mixture (mizaj). He presents three progressively stronger examples where we perceive whiteness even though no mixture is possible. These examples force the opponent to reconsider whether sensory perception is always reliable. In this lesson, we will trace his argument and understand the logical structure of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three examples: snow, powdered glass, and a crack in glass. The author claims each is a better example than the previous.
2
Understand the physical cause for snow
The author explains that snow's whiteness is due to light reflection between tiny transparent particles. This is a case where a physical process creates the perception of whiteness without actual white color.
The reflection of light is key.
3
Compare with powdered glass
Powdered glass appears white but has no mixture (mizaj) because its parts are hard, dry, and uniform. The whiteness cannot be explained by mixture, so sensory error is more evident.
No interaction between parts.
4
Consider the crack in glass
The crack appears white but only contains glass and air, neither colored. There are even no small particles, making it the strongest evidence of sensory error.
No parts to interact.
5
Reflect on the answer to doubts
The passage ends with an answer to potential doubts, stating that the implication of these examples is that sensory judgment about whiteness being a real color is mistaken. Consider how the author concludes that sensory perception is not always reliable.
The answer is incomplete in the source, but the direction is clear.

) وقولهم سببه ) أي سبب أنا نراه أبيض ( مداخلة الهواء ( المضيء بالأشعة الفائضة من الأجرام النيرة ( للأجزاء الشفافة) المتصغرة جداً ) وتعاكس الأضواء من سطوحها الصغار بعضها إلى بعض، فإن الضوء المنعكس يرى كلون البياض، ألا ترى أن الشمس إذا أشرقت على الماء وانعكس شعاعها منه إلى الجدار يرى الجدار كأنه أبيض فإذا كثر الانعكاس بين الأجزاء الرشية جداً تخيل ما على سطوحها من الضوء بیاضا في الغاية ( من النمط الأول ( أي من قبيل بيان أسباب الغلط، وقد عرفت أنه لا فائدة فيه على ما قرره ) وأظهر منه ) أي من الثلج في الدلالة على غلط الحس ( الزجاج المدقوق ) دقاً ناعماً فإنه يرى أبيض ولا بياض هناك (و) إنما كان أظهر لأنه (لم يحدث له مزاج يحدث ذلك المزاج (البياض) المشروط به عندهم ( فإن أجزاءه صلبة يابسة ) ومتفقة في الصور والكيفيات ( لا تفاعل بينها ) لعدم الالتصاق، واتفاق الضورة والكيفية فكيف يتصور حدوث المزاج فيه؟ مع كونه مشروطاً عندهم بالتفاعل وأما الثلج ففيه أجزاء مائية ،وهوائية، فجاز أن يتوهم فيما بينهما تفاعل (وأظهر منهما ) في الدلالة على غلط الحس (موضع الشق من الزجاج الثخين الشفاف) فإنه يرى أبيض ولا بياض هناك قطعا ( إذ ليس ثمة إلا الزجاج والهواء لمحتقن في ذلك الشق ( وشيء منهما غير ملون ) أي ليس شيء منهما بملون، وإنما كان أظهر منهما إذ ليس هناك أجزاء متصغرة يتوهم تفاعلها ( والجواب ) عن شبه هذه الفرقة ( أن مقتضاه ) أي مقتضى ما ذكرتم من الشبه الدالة على أن حكم
قوله: (المشروط به عندهم فإنهم ذهبوا إلى أن المزاج شرط في حدوث الألوان، ولا يحدث في البسائط .
قوله : (مع كونه مشروطاً الخ ) على ما هو المذهب المشهور، وإن ذهب بعض إلى أن التفاعل ليس بشرط بل مجرد الاجتماع في العناصر يوجب استعداد فيضان الكيفية المتوسطة التي هي المزاج، وإن لم يكن بينهن تفاعل في الكيفيات.
قوله : ( وأما الثلج الخ ( بخلاف تفاعل الأجزاء الزجاجية مع الأجزاء الهوائية بعد الدق، فإنه مستبعد لكونها صلة غير ملتصقة بالأجزاء الهوائية.

قوله : ( إذ ليس هاهنا أجزاء متصغرة وأما في الزجاج المدقوق ففيه تلك، ولهذا قيل إنها يسري فيها بعد الدق الهواء، ويحصل له مزاج آخر ، والصلابة غير مانعة من التفاعل.
And their statement: its cause — meaning the cause that we see it white — is the interposition of luminous air (with the rays overflowing from luminous bodies) in the very small transparent parts, and the reflection of lights from their small surfaces to one another. For the reflected light is seen as the color of whiteness. Do you not see that when the sun shines on water and its ray reflects from it onto a wall, the wall is seen as if it were white? And when the reflection is multiplied among the very fine particles, what is on their surfaces of light is imagined as extreme whiteness. (From the first type — i.e., from the category of explanation of the causes of error, and you have known that there is no benefit in it according to what he established. And more evident than it — i.e., than snow in indicating sensory error — is powdered glass ground finely, for it is seen white while there is no whiteness there. And it is more evident because there did not occur for it a mixture (mizaj) that would produce that whiteness which is condition for them, for its parts are hard, dry, and uniform in forms and qualities, with no interaction between them due to lack of adhesion and agreement of form and quality. So how can the occurrence of mixture in it be imagined, despite mixture being conditional for them upon interaction? As for snow, it contains watery and airy parts, so interaction between them could be imagined. And more evident than both of them in indicating sensory error is the place of a crack in thick transparent glass, for it is seen white while there is absolutely no whiteness there, since there is nothing but glass and the air confined in that crack, and neither of them is colored. And it is more evident than them because there are no small parts whose interaction could be imagined. (The answer) concerning the doubts of this group: that its implication — i.e., the implication of what you mentioned of the doubts indicating that the judgment...
His statement: (condition for them) for they held that mixture is a condition for the occurrence of colors, and it does not occur in simple substances.
His statement: (while it is conditional for them, etc.) according to the well-known doctrine, although some held that interaction is not a condition, but mere aggregation of elements necessitates a preparedness for the emanation of the intermediate quality which is mixture, even if there is no interaction in qualities between them.
His statement: (As for snow, etc.) unlike the interaction of glass particles with air particles after grinding, for it is unlikely due to their being solid and not attached to the air particles.
His statement: (conditional for them upon interaction) It is said: this is based on the well-known view; otherwise, some held that the juxtaposition of very small particles and their contact in specific positions prepares the stripping away of their contrary qualities and the acquisition of an intermediate quality from the source without interaction from them.
His statement: (for here there are no small parts) As for powdered glass, there are those, and it is said that air penetrates it after grinding and another mixture occurs for it, and hardness does not prevent interaction.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage explains the physical cause of the white appearance of snow and uses stronger examples to demonstrate sensory error. It states that the cause of seeing snow as white is the interposition of luminous air among the tiny transparent particles and the reflection of light from their small surfaces, so the reflected light is seen as white color. The analogy of sunlight reflecting on water and then on a wall is given. Then it presents a clearer example: finely powdered glass, which appears white with no whiteness present, because it has no mixture (mizaj) that would produce whiteness, as its parts are hard and dry with no interaction. In contrast, snow contains water and air parts, allowing possible interaction. An even clearer example is a crack in thick transparent glass, which appears white with no whiteness because only glass and air are present, neither colored. Then the passage gives the answer to the doubts of the skeptical group: the implication of these examples is that... (the answer is cut off). The marginal notes discuss different opinions on whether mixture (mizaj) is conditional for colors, and whether interaction is necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the whiteness of snow, powdered glass, or a glass crack is a real color caused by mixture (mizaj).
Correction: The author shows that these are optical effects of light reflection and that no mixture is present in the case of powdered glass and glass crack, proving sensory error.

D) Because glass reflects more light

Key Takeaway
The whiteness we perceive is not always a real color; sensory error is revealed when no physical cause (like mixture) can be identified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you trusted your eyes over something that later turned out to be an illusion? How does this lesson make you reconsider what you think is 'obvious' about the world around you?
?
The author argues that whiteness can exist without any white color. What beliefs do you hold so firmly that you would struggle to accept they might be based on sensory error? Will you examine them?
?
If even something as basic as seeing whiteness can be mistaken, how much more careful must you be about judging deeper matters of faith and character? What practical step will you take today to question a judgment you made about someone based on mere appearance?
